This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited
Brazil during July 1991. The mission comprised Messrs. B. Menezes (Task
Manager), J. Rebelo (Joint Task Manager) LAlIN; E. Abbott, LEGLA; J. Baigorria
(Sr. Railway Engineer) LATIE; P. Doty (Financial Advisor), L. Villela (State
Finances Consultant), R. Villela (Economist), J.L. Collard (Signalling and
Telecom Consultant), J. Kerr, (Rolling Stock and Workshops Consultant).
Messrs. R. Scurfield 'and L. Thompson (INUTD) acted as Peer Reviewers.  Mmes.
F. Cash and M. Torres helped prepare this report.
This project was prepared in parallel and appraised at the same time as a
proposed similar project in the State of Rio de Janeiro, which has been
postponed. Some data on RJ has been retained for comparative purposes.

M9TROPOLITAN TDMN$PORT   .CENTRALIZATION PROJECT IN SAO PAULO
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:         The Federative Republic of Brazil
Impl.muntina      The Brazilian Urban Train Company (CBTU) and the State of
Afencies:        of Sao Paulo
Amount:           US$126 million
Terms:            Repayment in 15 years, including five years of grace, at the Bank's
standard variable interest rate.
Objectives
and Descrigtion: The broad project objectives through a first project in Sao Paulo are
to lay the foundations for the financial, institutional and
organizational policy changes required to achieve sustainable,
integrated multimodal transport in ti.a SP MR. The specific objectives
for the proposed project are:
* to support the Federal Government in its efforts to transfer the
CBTU-SP subdivision to the State in order to enhance its management
and operations;
* to introduce institutional, organizational and financial policy
reforms to assure its long-term financial sustainability (including
cost-efficiency gains, improved multimodal integration, expanded
capacity, and substantial reduction in subsidies); and
- to contribute to poverty alleviation and environmental improvement
(facilitating access to work, decreasing their trip time to work, the
reduction of congestion, and the reduction of accidents and fatalities
through the provision of safor and more comfortable rail transport).
The model in this project will be utilized by CBTU to decentralize its
remaining 8 subsystems.
The project includes an Investment Component (IC) and an Institutional
Development (ID) component.   The IC component (90Z of the loan)
includes  civil  works  and  goods  to  implement  the  proposed
rehabilitation of stations, track, rolling stock, signalling, power
supply, telecommunications, fencing of the right of way, traffic
engineering equipment and construction of transfer points required to
integrate CBTU with other modes. The ID component (lOX of the loan)
will consist of the development and implementation of policies and
action programs to: a) establish organizational, financial and
institutional reforms as a basis for improved cost recovery, including
the implementation of a plan of action for the transfer of those
remaining viable CBTU subdivisions to their respective states;
(b) operationalize multi-modal and tariff integration between CBTU-SP,
PEPASA (Metropolitan Train) feeder bus systems and non-motorized
vehicles, introduce long run variable cost based fare structures which
reflect peak and off peak services and to improve targeting of



iv
subsidies; (c) identify an investment program to consolidate the
rehabilitation and decentralization in SP; (d) study and recommend
different  management  alternatives,   technical  improvements  and
financial reforms for improving the operating efficiency of the FEPASA
and the CMTC (SP), as well as similar studies for each of the
remaining operating subdivisions including a review of management
contracts and franchising or concessions to the private sector; and
(a) provide technical and management training to CBTU employees as
recommended in the organization and manpower development studies (and
after decentralization to the staff of each operating agency), and
staff of selected other transport agencies participating in the
project.
The project is estimated to require five years to implement, at a
total cost of US$281 million equivalent with a foreign exchange
component of US$126 million (45 percent). The project will be managed
by a special Program Implementation Unit (PIU), created in the CBTU
for the purpose of decentralization, of which the CBTU-SP will be the
first. It would remain active for the duration of the project. After
decentralization (12/31/93) the entire Unit will be transferred and
incorporated into the SP-Public Corporation to be set up for managing
system. This will ensure retention of project management experience
for continuity and consolidation.
?rol ct
Benefits:         Substantial quantifiable and non-quantifiable benefits are expected
from the provision of larger capacity and safer operation by CBTU,
namely, operating cost savings, time savings for the users, less
accidents and fatalities and employment generation through better
spatial integration of markets. In addition, there will be a positive
environmental impact through the reduction of fossil fuel consumption
and road congestion with a consequent improvement of air quality and
noise pollution, will require less ai,sel operated buses to the
electrified rail service cleaner and less noisy. Poverty alleviation
will also result from facilitation of access to employment. An
economic evaluation of the project shows an ERR of 46% for the
investment component which represents 96% of the total project cost.
Project
lisks:            The main risks involved are the dynamics and consequent delays in the
dialogue between the three levels of government, the political
reluctance to raising tariffs which will allow each sub-division to
reach the targeted working ratios, the possible delays in the
procurement process, and the timely availability of counterpart funds.
Negotiations between the Federal and State Governments have progressed
very well. All parties have agreed on the decentralization, and the
risks are more associated with "fine-tuning" its phasing and terms of
transfer. The tariff issue will remain a major risk although CBTU has
been increasing tariffs, because Governments may be tempted to freeze
tariffs for long periods of time, thereby eroding revenues. The
contract plans will contain provisions to protect the CBTU-SP system
from these imposed freezes.  The delays in the procurement process
will be minimized by requiring the borrower to produce the major
tender documents by Board Presentation. The timely availability of
counterpart funds will be addressed by seeking assurances that they
are included in the Federal Government FY92 provisions and subsequent
years, for CBTU.

I.  THE URBAN TRANSPORT SECT
A.   kackround
1.1 Urban Transportation is in a crisis in Brazil. Although the nation has
invested heavily in the sector over the past 15 years, the effective demand for
urban transport services particularly in major metropolitan regions (MRs),
increased by about 82Z in the last decade, and under present pricing policiee,
exceeds the existing peak hour supply in most NRs. To add to this capacity
shortage,    the   Brazilian   urban
transport    sector    suffers    from
institutional problems due to a lack    AVERAGE ANNUAL POPULATION GROWTH RATE
of  coordination  between  the  three       FOR BRAZIL AND S.PAULO METROP. REGION
levels of government responsible for         POPULATION GROWTH RAVE S
urban transport in the MRs. The New         . _         4.           4.0
Constitution  promulgated in October                         4.... .
1988, clearly transfers responsibility    4: I'  .      .........
for  urban  transport  to  state  and    3,            ......
municipal governments. The federally-    2.
owned  rail  system  (CBTU) has been .
financed and operated independently of l v        . o     1070 To la    1980 t1s8?
the transportation priorities within                       DECADE
MRs. This has led to inefficient modal  |Msuomn 90&PAULOMUNIClPLITY 
and tariff integration and, above all,  I                      -              -
to a lack of prioritization of urban   SOURCEEIPOT
transport investments. Finally, cost
recovery policies and mechanisms have  Figure 1
been so inadequate that some of the
systems (particularly the CBTU) have deteriorated to the extent that they are
no louger a safe and reliable means of transport.  The main objective of this
project is to support the Federal Governmment in its efforts to transfer the
CBTU-SP sub-division to the State of Sao Paulo in order to enhance its
operational and financial management.
1.2 The urban poor, who are the main users of the urban train system, bear the
brunt of these problems: (a) shortage of capacity at peak hours resulting in
overcrowded (>10 pass/m2) often inhuman conditions, (b) long work journeys (2.5
hours each way) from the metropolitan periphery to the urban centers, with often
more than two modal transfers; and (c) high proportion (20%) of their income
towards home to work fares (normally bus plus rail fares). Expansion of the
capacity utilization of the rail based systems has been singled out by several
studies, as the least-cost priority option to increase peak hour capacity in the
HRs especially of Sao Paulo (SP) and Rio de Janeiro (RJ), which between them
alone account for 65% of all urban transport trips. There exists a consensus,
that a decentralization of the CBTU systems ,i.e. the transfer of the
responsibility for the administration, operation, financing and ownership of the
CBTU systems in each of the 9 MRs, in line with the provisions of the New
Constitution, will make the systems more responsive to their ridership. This
decentralization should be coupled with the implementation of a comprehensive set
of efficiency-related policies which will, among other things, promote modal
integration, improve direct cost recovery, target subsidies, and institutionalize
a predictable and sustainable system for financing the operating shortfall of
each MR operating agency, and for expansion of the systems in the future.
1.3  Urban Growth Fuels Demand for Urban Transport.   While the national
population almost tripled, from 52 million in 1950 to 150.5 million in 1990, the

their work places.
B. The Institutional
Framework
1.4 The institutional framework for urban transport policy and financing has
(in common with many sectors in Brazil), been considerably weakened 4n the recent
past. State and municipalities, traditionally responsible for the provision of
urban infrastructure and services, became increasingly unable to meet their
growing financial obligations.
Federal: In 1964, the Federal Government (GOB) centralized policy and
investment decisions by creating the National Housing Bank (BNH) to provide
financing for a broad range of shelter-related services.  More than a decade
later, in response to growing transport problems, the Brazilian Urban Transport
Company (EBTU) was established in 1976 to implement urban transport policy and
to finance urban transport investments.   At the same time,  the Brazilian
Transport Planning Agency (GEIPOT) was created to assist preparation of both
urban and inter-urban transport policies and investment plans.  In 1984, the
Brazilian Urban Rail Company (CBTU) was formed in an effort to separate
nationwide rail-based goods and commuter services. The CBTU was empowered to take
over the planning, operation and financing of the majority of suburban rail
systems, focused in the MRs. From 1979 to 1985, the National Urban Development
Council (CNDU) had the primary responsibility for urban development coordination
within the GOB and with other levels of Government. In March 1985, the Ministry
of Urban Development and the Environment (MDU) was established and took over
CNDU, BNH and (in January 1986) EBTU; the urban divisions of GEIPOT were also

1.5  Given the context of the rapidly
changing institutional scenario, there



-4-
is an urgent need for reforms to: (a) define the specific roles and
responsibilities for urban transport sector within the GOB, at the moment, split
between the Ministry of Infrastructure (for the CBTU) and the Ministry of Social
Action (MAS) for road based transport; (b) improve the interaction between the
three levels of government in urban transport currently weak or non-existentI and
(c) strengthen local coordination, especially in the MRs, to assure proper
planning, programming, coordination and monitoring of the urban transport
services.   The lack of sowe formal coordination entity leads to poor modal
integration, a duplication of investment, and wastage of scarce resources.
C.  The eVCoatiti             I
1.6 While the institutional framework is weak, the environment for introducing
aerious and long-term reforms, is stronger than it has ever been, especially as
a consequence of the provisions under Brazil's New Federal Constitution enacted
in 1988, which shifted the responsibilities for urban transport from the GO0 to
the states and municipalities. It stipulates, among other things, that  the
Federal Government would have responsibility of enacting legislation concerning
guidelines in national transport policy; and for transit and transportation.
However, the Federal Goverrnent can authorize the states to legislate on specific
questions related to those guidelines.   Such legislation would define the
contractual terms (concessions) for public service companies, special conditions
and their terms, renewal, including supervision. Such legislation will also
define the rights of the users, the tariff policy and the obligaticin of
maintaining an adequate level of service.
1.7 For sub-national governments, the Constitution authorizes states to organize
themselves, and observe their own charter and laws, as long as the principles
stated in the Federal Constitution are observed. States are allowed to designate
metropolitan and microregions and urban conglomerates, by grouping the
neighboring municipalities, in order to integrate the planning and execution of
public serving common interest. Furthermore, the Constitution states that it is
the responsibility of the municipalities to: enact legislation on matters of
local interest; organize and provide for, directly or under a concession, always
through bidding, the public services of local interest, including public
transport, which is considered an essential service.
1.8  The 1988 Constitution also mandated fiscal reform transferring a significant
larger proportion of tax revenues from GOB to subnational governments,
facilitating the latter to assume the financial responsibilities which accompany
the decentralization of services.
D. Key Issues in the Urban Transport Sector
A recent Bank sector review identified 3 sets of crucial issues in which
to support policy reforms: (a) institutional; (b) financial and economic; and
(c) technical.
1.9 The first set of issues include:
Fine-Tuning  Intergovernmental  Relations:  geared  towards  a clear
definition of their respective roles in the financing, planning and
operation of urban transport services in accordance with the 1988
Constitution;



ll: ~ ~   ~      ~      ~     ~      5
*     Creation of a Coordination Agency In each MRs formally empowered for
planning, coorditijtin6 and setting priorities for new multi-year
investments in order to (i) assur:e a minimum of planning continuity,
and to encourage modal integration including tariff setting and cost
sharing policies; and (ii) strengthening the technical capacity for
transportation planning, and the economic and financial evaluation of
new investments especial.,  siith the proliferation of the proposed high
rail transit (LRT) systet.  and
*     Introducing Regulatory Reform: targeted towards (i) the elimination
of regulatory barriers which might   prevent  free entry  and/or
competition in the market for provision of transport services
especially bus services; (ii) thie encouragement of market based
incentives for pollution control to the extent possible, to minimize
the fiscal burden implied by de facto government subsidies to
polluters; and (Iii) allocating responsibilities for the enforcement
of the law and definition of tougher standards, across government
levels
1.10 The second set of issues woxuld require a reform of current financial and
ecoaomic policy and management towards addressing short term (operations and
maintenance) and long term (investment) requirements. Specifically these should
include:
*     Cost Recovery and Tariff Policies: targeted towards improving the
financial viability of operating entities by (i) setting tariffs
which, when added to subsidies, cover at least the long-run variable
costs (defined as out-of-pocket costs plus depreciation of equipment
and cost of capital) of the service provided and by controlling fare
evasion; (ii) moderating demand through setting of appropriate peak
and off-peak pricing;
*     Improving the Targeting of Urban Transport Subsidies: to the low
income population, especially to the informal sector. Existing
mechanisms such as the "vale de transporte" are intended to provide
such subsidies but are only available to those in formal employment.
Only 40% of the target population for which thils subsidy was designed
takes advantage of it. In this subsidy, the employer picks up all the
urban transport costs   above 6% of its employees' salary.  He is
allowed to deduct these costs from his Federal Income Tax.
Unfortunately, a good percentage of the target population does not
have formal employees and therefore cannot take advantage of this
ingenious targeted subsidy;
d     Imlproving the Financial Management of Public-Owned Systems: through
wide ranging cost cutting measures, staff rationalization policies,
strict adherence to operating targets through the introduction of
Contract Plans, and by appointing more professional managers to run
the mass transit systems; and
*     Revamping of Funding Mechanisms  and Policies:  in  order  to  (i)
guarantee adequate financing of subsidies on a predictable basis
(tariff differentiation, contracted budgetary transfers, etc.) and
channelling them (tokens, multimodal passes, differential tariffs);



-6 -
and (ii)) generate resources for the implementation or extension of
mass transit sys'cems.
1.11 The third set of technical issues relate to:
Capacity and Safety:   the need to increase the supply of adequate
cost-efficient peak hour capacity in order to guarantee an acceptable
level of service to the average commuter, under reasonable safety
conditions. This can be done by (i) rebabilitating and restructuring
existing systems, implementing modal integration, and by selective
expansion of new services which prove to be the most efficient
alternative from the technical, economic and, especially, financial
standpoint; (ii) improving traffic management to maximize use of
available  road  space  and  reduce  congestion,  through  route
rationalization for mass transport services, through appropriate
traffic engineering equipment and low enforcement; (iii) reducing
traffic and transit accidents through a combination of transit safety
education campaigns, law enforcement and appropriate traffic
engineering;  and  (iv)  exploring  and  implementing  alternative
technologies to minimize environmental problems.
E. The Ba s Strategy for the Subsector
1.12 Urban transport policies would be geared toward the efficient provision of
the urban transport services which are required to restore the economic
efficiency of cities, and which improve access to affordable transportation
through directly targeted subsidies, the low-income populations which have
traditionally received less attention. rolicies would be consistent with the
broader transport sector objectives, as well as with the objectives of the urban
sector, and of poverty alleviation.
1. 13 In view of tbe goals stated above, which encompass economic efficiency, cost
recovery, selective targeting of subsidies to alleviating poverty, administrative
convenience and modal coordination, the Bank's vision for the sector in the
short-term (3-5 years) is oriented towards: (i) assisting the three levels of
government in formulating and implementing an urban transport policy consistent
with the increased role of subonational governments; (ii) supporting an emergency
program to rehabilitate existing services such as thLe CBTU rail services, metro
lines and busways, which can easily and quickly increase their efficiency and
their peak hour capacity; (iii) introducing better cost recovery mechanisms
including adequate cost-based peak hour tariffs and fare evasion control in the
rehabilitated services in order to strengthen their financial base; (iv)
increasing the efficiency and equity of existing services through multimodal
tariff and physical integration; (v) promote deregulation and private sector
participation; and (vi) improving the targeting of subsidies to the poor.
1.14 Over the medium term (5-7 years), the Bank's support is expected to shift
towards a focus on promoting a more efficient and financially suotainable market
oriented supply of public transport services, leading to a final phase (> 7
years) of policy consolidation.
F. Past ExRerience in the Sector: Lessons for the Future



-7
1.15 Bank support to the urban transport sector has been provided through four
urban transport projects totalling US$51:3 million, of which the First, Second and
Third Urban Transport Projects (Loans 1563-BR, 1839-BR, 1965-BR) were completed.
The IV Urban Transport Project (Loan 2822-BR) which continued the policy
initiated in the Third project, was cancelled when the GOB abolished EBTU.
1.16 The major lessons learned from previous urban transport sector loans are
described in detail in ANNEX 1 and can be summarized as follows: (a) because of
the complex institutional framework outlined earlier (see para. 1.4), the
coordination between the three levels of government in urban transport cannot be
assumed, and must be dealt with very carefully at the project preparation level
and during project supervision. Institutional arrangements and agreements should
be reviewed in detail during a mid-term project review (para. 3.23); (b) policy
reform must be directed towards promoting modal integration, in order to minimize
distortions resulting from inefficient physical and financial coordination
between modes; (c) the tariff levels should allow for significant cost recovery
o' working costs and must be complemented by pre-defined financing mechanisms
which cover the shortfall between operating costs and revenues; (d) demand levels
forecast by the borrowers should be carefully scrutinized, and (e) more attention
must be given to issues of maintenance/management of infrastructure.
II. RAIL TRANSPORT IN THE URBAN TRANSPORT SECTOR:
THE ROLE OF CBTU
A. The Creation and Role of CBTU
2.1 Suburban rail services are provided in several of the MRs either by the
Brazilian Urban Railways Transport Company (CBTU) or by State oxwned entities such
as FEPASA in Sao Paulo and TRENSTURB in Porto Alegre. GOB created the Brazilian
Urban Rail Company (CBTU) in 1984, by separating suburban rail passenger services
from freight services which, at that time, were both being provided by the
national railways (RRFSA) on existing rail lines (810 km), in 9 states. GOB's
objective was to create an agency fully dedicated to suburban rail service which
could offer a more cost efficient rail service to the MRs than that provided by
the Federal Railways (REFSA), thereby relieving some of the MRs peak-hour
capacity problems. CBTV operates suburban rail services in Rio ee Janeiro (380
km), Sao Paulo (192 km), Recife (52.5 km), Belo Horizonte (33.6 km), Salvador
(13.5 km), Fortaleza (42 km), Macei6 (33 km), Joao Pessoa (30 km), and Natal (56
km). The CBTU's 9 subdivisions cover 44 municipalities with a total population
of 38 million inhabitants, and provide daily transportation service to about 1.9
million passengers. However, it carries less than half the passengers it was
expected to serve, offers a very poor level of service, requires an average
subsidy of US$700 million/year from the GOB and is not reaching the modal share
initially targeted for the system (15%). This target could be easily achieved
if it were operating properly. CBTU is therefore, falling short of the goals for
which it was created.
2.2   Although  CBTU carries less than 8% of all motorized trips in the MRs, it
serves the most poor areas of the MRs, penetrates most city centers and has an
estimated repressed demand (simply due to lack of capacity as present prices),
in both Sao Paulo and Rio of an estimated 1,000,000 pass/day each. This is the
result of an inefficient operation due to frequent disruptions of the system
caused by equipment shortages and failures, power supply and overhead catenary
problems, lack of appropriate signalling to decrease headways and frequent



accidents in the rail right of way. The weak financial situation of CBTU is a
result of poor cost recovery mechanisms such as very low tariffs (normally 1/3
of tho bus tariffs for equivalent trip lengths), fare evasion, a non-commercial
oriented management, lack of appropriate cost accounting and financir.1
managemenit, very weak inventory management which affects spare part and rolling
stock availability and inexistent staff and line rationalization strategies. All
of CBTU's subdivisions are being examined in the light of present and potential
ridership, and replacement by more cost effective modes is being evaluated where
appropriate.

making the CBTU sustainable must be linked to its decentralization to improve,
among other things, the institutional framework for interaction between the three
levels of government, modal integration, and financial management as discussed
below.
C. The Institutional Constraints of the CBTU
2.4 Over the years, CBTU operations (under federal jurisdiction) have become
increasingly distant from the needs of its ridership and its management had both
insufficient resources and lack of autonomy to improve the quality of service
offered. Operational policy, administrative directives, personnel management,
and most important, the allocation of short and long term financial resources,
are dictated from a distance and inevitably fluctuate with the changing
political, economic and financial scenarios of incoming and outgoing federal
administrations. Every year industrial action by striking unions, or physical
breakdowns of CBTU's systems in Rio and Sao Paulo alone result in paralyzing rail



- 9 -
transport, with consequent chaos in road based public transport and traffic
management in the MRs, leading to significant losses in economic productivity.
Since May  1990,  CBTU has been under  a new management and  considerable
improvements have been made (see para 2.9).   However, these gains will be
shortlived unless the institutional framework is changed to improve the
interaction and "modus-operandill between the three levels of government, within
the context of supporting policy reforms and the plant and equipment are
rehabilitated to provide an acceptable level-of-service to the main user.

2.5 There is a general consensus for the need of a three-pronged approach to
(a) decentralize the responsibility for policy and operations of the CBTU to the
local level as mandated by the Constitution; (b) set up a Local Coordination
Agency (LCA) i  each MR, recognized by all levels of government, and with the
authority and also the means to promote and ensure modal integration; and (c)
introduce appropriate reforms (such as efficient pricing, lack of entry barriers
and other deregulation). A well managed decentralized CBTU can be sustainable,
if the LCA is effective. Ideally, the LCA should be a consensus building body
which prioritizes investments, promotes modal integration and allocates subsidies
in a quasi deregulated environment, and not a command/control agency. The LCA
would have representatives from each level of government, the operators, and the
users. Furthermore, this agency would have the responsibility to advise and
recommend the allocation of resources in response to market forces and within
specific targeted poverty alleviation objectives. This type of coordinating
entity exists in major cities throughout the world but not in Brazil. Hitherto,
the main barrie;s to the creation of such entities in Brazil, have been the
reluctance at each level of government, to give up traditional control of
investment resource allocation within their respective jurisdictional areas.
The absence of such LCAs to plan, evaluate and prioritize urban transport sector
policy investments in the MRs, is also the main obstacle to effective physical
and tariff modal integration.



- 10 -

modes except in Sao Paulo where a major effort promoted and sustained by the
METRO has resulted in good physical and tariff integration between the several
modes. The construction of well located transfer terminals was the catalyst for
such an efficient integration. The next step for Sao Paulo must be to consolidate
the policy coordination and

transport systems serving the MRs ought to be a top priority in the sector and
the proposed project includes several measures to assure better integration of
CBTU with the other modes.
E. Present Financial Situation of CBTU
2.7 As in most urban rail systems throughout the world, CBTU requires, at
present, massive annual subsidies to operate (average US$708 million/year during
the 1988-1990 period).  The shortfall in CBTU revenues is mainly due to low
tariffs, which are lower than its working costs and are Government imposed for
"social"' reasons; and also to high tariff evasion (almost llZ of ridership) due
to lack of adequate control at stations.  Until recently, CBTU tariffs were
controlled by the Federal Government and in general, were 1/3 of the urban bus
1J While modal integration is desirable and recommended, it can only succeed in increasing
ridership if the user sees considerable financial advantages in utilizing the combined transport
option, and the bus operators see financial advantages in serving the rail stations, and the
regulatory agency encourages such integration.



- 11 -
tariffs in the MR. Unlike CBTU, the urban bus tariff, the intermunicipal bus
tariffs and the metro and FEPASA tariffs are set by either the municipality or
the State. The FEPASA metropolitan train (state owned) charges tariffs which
are, traditionally, 2 to 3 times higher than CBTU's tariff, but it offers a
better level of service. Based on recent train surveys of the CBTU ridership,
the latter will be willing to pay a tariff comparable to the one charged by
FEPASA, if the service improves.
2.8 In 1990, while CBTU farebox revenue was US$0.11/passenger, its working
cost/passenger, i.e., the cost, not including depreciation and cost of capital,
was US$0.96. The ratio between working costs and operating revenues (working
ratio) was respectively 9.2, 14.8 and 8.7 in 1988, 1989 and 1990. Table 2.2
shows condensed income statements for CBTU as a whole for 1988, 1989 and 1990.
It also shows a breakdown of Government subsidies to the system.  During the
1988-1990 period, the Federal Government subsidized 1002 of working costs, 100%
of capital improvements (such as new equipment), and 100% of the debt service.
It covered, however, only 80% of the operating costs because it did not pay for
all the depreciation and interest costs.
2.9 The total subsidy per paying passenger in the CBTU system ranged from $1.50
to $1.37 from 1988 to 1990. In August 1991, the Government liberated the tariffs
and is allowing CBTU to adjust them more in line with the other transport modes,
reserving, however, the right of an ex-post review. Furthermore, CBTU has also
been allowed to practice different tariffs in different subdivisions instead of
a single tariff across all subdivisions. CBTU new management also embarked on
a cost reduction program including a staff rationalization program, which reduced
personnel from 22,900 to 17,600 since March 1990.
2.10 The improvement of CBTU's financial situation can only be achieved through
a combination of higher paying passenger demand, tariffs which are as close to
working costs as possible, and a comprehensive cost reduction program.   The
higher demand exists since there is, at present, repressed demand.  However, to
materialize this demand and to increase revenues, a better and more reliable
level of service is required and tariff evasion must be reduced or eliminated.
This can only be achieved byJ a rehabilitation of track and stations, equipment
and systems. The higher tariffs, closer to working costs, are possible and in
the last year, the tariff has advanced from 10% to 20% of working costs.
Although this is a substantial increase, it is far from what is necessary to
decrease dependency on subsidies (paras. 4.10-4.15). Tariffs should progress,
as fast as they can, to 60-70% of an acceptable working cost. However, it is
recognized that, there are affordability issues which are discussed in detail in
Annex 4B.   Notwithstanding those issues,  a higher tariff is possible and
desirable. Finally, a comprehensive cost reduction program is already underway
and requires additional staff cuts, better inventory management and improved
preventive maintenance. All these aspects are taken into account in the proposed
Project.

F.  De  ntralization of CBTU
2.11 In summary, since the mid 1980's, the CBTU has lost more than 1 million
passengers/day in its Rio and S. Paulo subdivisions which, at present, are only
transporting 1.5 million passengers/day. This is the result of an inefficient
operation due to frequent disruptions of the system caused by equipment shortages
and failures, power supply and overhead catenary problems, lack of appropriate
signalling to decrease headways and frequent accidents which disrupt rail
sezA.ices. The deferred maintenance pattern experienced by CBTU is due to poor
maintenance policies and a weak financial situation resulting from: (a) a heavy
dependence on the cash-strapped federal government for operating subsidies; (b)
poor cost recovery mechanisms such as very low tariffs and fare evasion; (c) a
lack of appropriate cost accounting and financial management; (d) very weak
inventory management which affects spare part and rolling stock availability and
(e) non-existent staff and line rationalization strategies.
2.12 The GOB has decided to transfer sub-divisions of the CBTU to the respective
States and has set itself a December 1993 deadline to accomplish this goal. To
facilitate this decentralization, it has decided to embark on an urgent physical
rehabilitation and financial restructuring program for the CBTU-SP and thereafter
the CBTU-RJ subdivision, through which it intends to improve overall operational
performance and, reduce operating expenses, and has approached the Bank for
financial and technical support. The rationale for such strategy is that CBTU's
transfer to the States will allow local Governments direct participation in their
planning and operations, better monitoring and integration with other urban
transport services under their jurisdiction in the MRs and, consequently, will
lead to cost-efficiency gains which, when combined with policy and tariff
reforms, will improve the level of service and produce a substantial reduction
of government subsidies to rail transport.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Proiect Objectives and Rationale
3.1 The broad objectives of the project are to lay the foundations for the
financial, institutional and organizational policy changes required to achieve
sustainable, integrated multimodal transport in the Metropolitan Regions of
Brazil. The specific objectives for the proposed first CBTU project are: (a) to
support the Federal Government in its efforts to transfer the CBTU-SP subdivision
to the State of Sao Paulo in order to enhance its management and operations; (b)
to strengthen institutional, organizational and financial policy reforms in the
CBTU-SP to assure its long-term financial sustainability (including crst-
efficiency gains, improved multimodal integration, expanded capacity, and
substantial reduction in subsidies); and (c) to contribute to poverty alleviation
and environmental improvement (facilitating access to work, decreasing trip time
to work, the reduction of congestion, and the reduction of accidents and
fatalities through the provision of safer and more comfortable rail transport).
3.2 The Bank's assistance strategy in Brazil is to support policies and
investments that will encourage economic growth and social development in a
context of macroeconomic stability.   Our emphasis is on efficient resource
allocation, increased efficiency in the public sector and the appropriate
targeting and delivery of support systems to the poor.  The proposed project



- 14 
objectives are in line with these objectives, namely: (a) to promote financial
viability of public enterprises and their reform, including decentralization to
various levels; (b) to contribute to poverty alleviation; and (c) to reduce
Government subsidies through better cost recovery policies. From a multimodal
perspective and given the forecast peak hour demand, the average trip length and
the cost of alternative modal options,   CBTU's rehabilitation will produce
quicker and higher benefits than projects in other modes.  Furthermore, the
proposed project is in agreement with the GOB's stated desire to transfer urban
transport to thle states and municipalities and improve the efficiency of delivery
of urban transportation services. Through its involvement in the preparation of
this project, the Bank has already helped in accelerating the decentralization
process and will facilitate the process of cormpletion. At the same time, it will
stimulate a thorough review and improvements of sub-sector policies--particularly
cost-recovery, financing and modal integration--at the federal, state and
municipal levels.
B.  Project Description
3.3 The project includes an Investment Ccmponent (IC) and an Institutional
Development (ID) component. The IC component (90% of the loan) includes civil
works and goods to implement the proposed rehabilitation of stations, track,
rolling stock, signalling, power supply, telecommunications, fencing of the right
of way, traffic engineering equipment and construction of transfer points
required to integrate CBTU with other modes. The ID component (10% of the loan)
will consist of the development and implementation of policies and action
programs to: a) establish organizational, financial and institutional reforms as
a basis for improved cost recovery, including the implementation of a plan of
action for the transfer of those remaining viable CBTU subdivisions to their
reGspective states; (b) operationalize multi-modal and tariff integration between
CBTU-SP, FEPASA (Metropolitan Train) feeder bus systems and non-motorized
vehiclees, introduce long run variable cost based fare structures which reflect
peak and off peak services and to improve targeting of subsidies; (c) identify
an investment program to consolidate the rehabilitation and decentralization in
SP; (d) study and recommend different management alternatives, technical
improvements and financial reforms for improving the operating efficiency of the
FEPASA and the CMTC (SP), as well as similar studies for each of the remaining
operating subdivisions including a review of management contracts and franchising
or concessions to the private sector; and (e) provide techlnical and management
training to CBTU employees as recommended in the organization and manpower
development studies (and after decentralization to the staff of each operating
agency), and staff of selected other transport agencies participating in the
project.
C. Proiect Comionents
The Policy and Institutional Development Action Plan
3.4 CBTU's decentralization is not by itself a guarantee that the individual
subdivisions will be better managed than its predecessors.  A package   of
institutional and policy reforms are needed to induce modernization changes and
motivate management and staff, but also the financial management and
administration, especially in regard to cost recovery mechanisms of the system.
Otherwise, the vicious cycle for another emergency program of rehabilitation
will repeat itself with all the negative impacts on the ridership and on the MR



- 15 -
as a whole. Furthermore, actions are needed to change the environment in which
CBTU operates in order to facilitate the modal integration and sustainability
desired in the long term.  The basic sub-components of the institutional and
policy package proposed are described in detail in Annex 3A and are summarized
belowt
(i)     Decentralization:  The first set of strategic Actions Plans concerns
the  actual   decentralization  process:   (a)   institutional   and
organizational arrangements; (b) patrimonial-agreement on the
valuation and assets to transferred; (c) aspects relating to transfer
of personnel, pension benefits, and miscellaneous human resource-
related   issues;   (d)   legal  and   administrative   aspects   for
decentralization; (e) financial and economic aspects including a study
of short and long-term financial impacts of the transfer; (f)
engineering-focused on a detailed hand-over timetable of rehabilitated
rail sections; (g) physical integration - with on-going infrastructure
projects by the host municipalities (including the construction of
some bus terminals with the participation of the private sector, and
bicycle storage facilities).
(ii)     Management:  The second set of Action Plans are designed to improve
the management of the new CBTU subdivisions: (a) preparation and
implementation of a manpower development plan, to streamline the
management and operations staff in each of the new subdivisions, to
propose concise job descriptions and to staff them accordingly; (b)
a cost accounting, management information systems and financial
management study and implementation so that timely and adequate cost
management is possible; (c) preparation of an action plan to improve
inventory management since lack of spares is one of the main causes
for the low rolling stock availability rates; (d) preparation of an
action plan to subcontract maintenance and other operations to the
private sector; (e) preparation of an action plan to increase non-
operating revenues by renting station space, advertising and
development of subdivision's real estate; (f) technical assistance and
training for staff at all levels as proposed in the manpower plan.
(iii)     Modal Integration:  The objective of the third set of Action Plans is
to     support  the  policy  framework,  and  provide  practical
recommendations for implementing modal integration. These studies will
also include a review of existing transport agencies with a view to
reducing cross-inefficiencies across modes: (a) a tariff study focused
on  achieving  multi-modal   tariff   integration,   including   its
operationalization; (b) updating of the Transport portion of the
Master Plans of the City of SP with emphasis on route rationalization
modal and tariff integration; (c) CMTC management and route
rationalization study; (d) FEPASA's operations and management and
integration study.
Some of these studies will be combined. This component will also include for
training and technical assistance of the relevant sector staff of the CBTU-SP,
and staff of participating agencies in the LCA, as set out in Annex 3A.



16 -
The Rehabilitation Progratu
3.5 This program is designed to rehabilitate the infrastructure and equipment
of CBTU-SP to a level which will allow it to meet the operational and financial
targets indicated in para. 3.22, and can be summarized as follows:
SAO PAULO SYSTEM (CBTU-SP)
3.6  The track rehabilitation program would consist of: (a) infrastructure works
including improvement of drainage systems on about 13 line-km, the rehabilitation
of 6 small bridges and 43 m of culverts, the construction of 3 and rehabilitation
of 4 pedestrian overpasses, the construction of about 1,200 M2 of retaining walls
and of 6,800 m of fences along the right of way; (b) superstructure works
including the replacement of sleepers, fastenings, ballast and rail switches,
on specific sections, for a total of about 244 line-km (Santos-Jundiat, Leste
main and Leste bypass corridors)t(AP IBRD 23134); and (c) rehabilitation of track
maintenance equipment and related spare parts for maintenance. The passenger
stations program would consist of the modernization/rehabilitation of 19 stations
including fences.
3.7 The rolling stoc'x program would consist of: (a) the rehabilitation of 12
EMUs; (b) the modernization of 37 ENUs and (c) the acquisition of eight second
hand and/or remanufactured diesel locomotives.   The workshop program would
consist of: (a) the construction of a small depot for maintenance of heavy track
equipment  (Lapa), and of a depot for the mat-tenance of EMUs;           (b) the
acquisition of plant equipment for workshops; and (c) the construction of a
laboratory for the repair and testing of electrical components, including related
plant equipment.
3.8 The signalling program would consist of: (a) reinstalling track circuits
on 10 track sectors which cover about 31 km of line in order to decrease the
headway; (b) acquiring 51 point machines to modify some installations and provide
a standby reserve; (c) installing signalling in 2 lines with the RFFSA network;
(d) equipping about 85 km of line with ATS; (e) replacing 54 km of cable; and (f)
renewing the centralized traffic control systems on about 130km of line. The
telecommunications program will consist of: (a) installing an integrated
telecommunications system on the overall network as well as on the secondary
cable network; (b) installing a track telephone system, a track-to-train radio
system and indication panels in 14 stations; (c) installing train loudspeaker
systems in 26 stations and on 114 trains.  The electrification program will
consist in repairing approximately 940 overhead contact line attachments and to
improve the distribution by installing 31 network switching devices.
D. Proiect Organization and Imiplementation
3.9 The project will be managed by a special Project Implementation Unit (PIU),
created in CBTU for managing the execution of the project (see Chart 2). CBTU and
the State agreed that the PIU will continue to manage the execution of the
project after the Operational Transfer of the CBTU-SP system to the State. This
will ensure the continuity of project management experience acquired prior to
Operational Transfer and will prevent delays which could occur due to an eventual
change of the project implementation team before the conclusion of the project.
To manage and guide the "non-hardware" aspects (political, legal, administrative,
institutional, and so forth), through the transition and until December 1993, an



- 17 
intergovernmental General Coordination Committee will coordinate the work of a
number of task forces, which will carry out strategic studies on terms of
reference agreed with the bank during negotiations.
3.10 Actual execution of the various action programs would be the responsibility
of CBTU's respective Directorates, with support from CBTU-SP and with appropriate
external technical assistance. The Federal Government through CBTU, the State
of SP through its State Secretary of Metropolitan Transport and, in some cases,
the Municipality of SP, will participate jointly in most of the action programs.
A separate and independent Monitoring and Audit consultant will be contracted,
reporting directly to the President of CBTU.
3.11 During loan negotiations, agreement was reached on: (a) project staffing
of the PIU which will include a unit chief with project management and
engineering experience in projects of this type, a procurement specialist, a
financial analyst and a transport systems planner; (b) on the type of project
management information system (PMIS) package required to monitor the execution
of the project and the type of reports to be submitted to the Bank ; and on the
Implementation Program which is a schedule of the CBTU Project Agreement.
E. Im,lementation Schedule
3.12 The GOB and the State of SP have given high priority to this project, and
its implementation has been scheduled over a five-year period (1992-1996),
compared with the Bank's average disbursement profiles for similar projects (6.5
years). An overall implementation schedule and a specific procurement schedule
are shown in Annex 3F. Key events leading to decentralization are scheduled as
shown in Chart 1.
3.13 CBTU's management believes it has the technical capabilities and expertise
required to implement the project during a shorter period than proposed by the
Bank, i.e. 1992-1994, subject to the availability of counterpart funds. An
alternative cost-table for such accelerated option is shown in Annex 7. Provided
the Bank's guidelines for procurement are followed, the Bank would not have any
objection to a shorter implementation period and in accelerating the disbursement
schedule.

US$167.6 million or about 59.6% of total cost. Taxes and duties are expected to
account for about 14% of total costs or US$39.2 million equivalent.   Total
project cost includes US$12.0 million for the policy and institutional
development action plan, and US$269.0 million for the rehabilitat-!on program
(table on the next page and tables Ti to T5 at back of SAR).   Physical
contingencies add up to US$23.2 million, or 9.6% of the base cost,, and price
contingencies are estimated at US$17.1 million or 6.5% of the base cost plus

3.17 Procurement procedures would be as follows:
(a) contracts for goods estimated to cost more than US$300,000 equivalent
each, and contracts for civil works including systems, and mechanical works
estimated to cost more than US$5.0 million equivalent each, would be
procured through ICB procedures in accordance with Bank's guidelines. In
ICB procurement of goods, local bidders would. be granted a margin of
preference by adding 15% (or the applicable custom duties, whichever is
lower)  to the CIF value of the foreign bids21.   A'though it is not
foreseen, the procurement of any goods below US$300,000 may eventually be
procured through LCB procedures up to an aggregate amount of US$1.5
million; (b) civil works of US$5.0 million equivalent or less, up to an
aggregate amount of US$24.5 million (Table 3.2) would be awarded through
local competitive bidding procedures and foreign bidders would have the
opportunity to compete; in additiorn, prequalification procedures would be

followed for the procurement of all contracts for civil works,

telecommunications (rolling stock and systems being turn-key jobs); and
(c) consultants, which are expected to aggregate to about US$19.8 million
(including supervision of investment under the project), would be selected
and engaged following the Bank's "Guidelines for the use of Consultants"
(August 1981).
3.18 All contracts for goods estimated to cost the equivalent of US$300,000 or
more, and all contracts for works estimated to cost the equivalent of US$5.0
million or more, which overall represent about 83% of all Bank-financed goods and
civil works, would be subject to the Bank's prior review of the procurement
documentation. CBTU's management has the capabilities to handle adequately the
procurement processes required for this project. During appraisal, agreement was
reached with CBTU: (i) on the use of standard bidding documents for the
procurement of goods and/or civil works, under LCB and ICB procedures
satisfactory to the Bank, and of the guidelines for the selection of consultants;
a standard letter of invitation and contract will be agreed at negotiations; and
(ii) that no later than January 1, 1992, CBTU would send to the Bank for review
a complete set of bidding documents to be used as samples for all ICB (in
english) and LCB tenders under the project. The two firet LCB contracts before
and after decentralization takes place, regardless of value, will be reviewed by
the Bank. Procurement information will be collected and recorded as follows:
(a)     prompt reporting of contract award information by the borrower;
(b)     comprehens re quarterly reports to the Bank by the borrower (assisted
by connultants), indicating:
*    revised cost estimates for individual contracts and the total project,
including best estimates of allowances for physical and price
contingency;
*    revised timing of procurement actions, including advertising, bidding,
contract award, and completion time for individual contracts;
*    compliance with aggregate limits on specified methods of procurement;
and
(b)     a completion report by the borrower within three months of loan's
closing date.
H. Disbursement. Accounts and Audits
3.19 Disbursements of the Bank loan have been scheduled over a five year period
(1992-1996) (Table T6 "disbursements"). Within the 45Z level of cost-sharing,
the Bank loan would be disbursed for: (a) works: (i) civil works (rehabilitation
of track infra/superstructure, stations, workshop buildings, electrification,
signalling and telecommunications, at a rate of 40Z of total expenditures; and
(ii) rolling stock (rehabilitation and modernization of EMUs and wagons) at a
rate of 56% of total expenditures; (b) goods (purchases of impregnated timber,
fastenings, turno'uts, rail welding portions, insulated joints and track
maintenance and workshop equipment) at a rate of 1002 of CIF costs of foreign
expenditures or, for bids awarded to local manufacturers, 100% of local
expenditures (ex-factory costs excluding taxes); (c) tecmnical assistance; and



- 22 -
(d) training of personnel, at a rate of 50% of local consultants residing in
Brazil and for training in Brazil, or 100X of foreign expenditures for other
consultants and for training abroad.
3.20 In order to reduce the time period during which CBTU would pre-finance the
Bank's share of project costs, two Special Accounts in US dollars, with initial
deposits of US$10.0 million and US$1.0 million respectively, would be established
in the Central Bank and in a bank, acceptable to the Bank, for the foreign and
local expenditures respectively. Withdrawals of expenditures against contracts
for goods estimated to cost the equivalent of US$300,000 or more, and all
contracts for works estimated to cost the equivalent of US$5.0 million would be
made on the basis of Statements of Expenditure (SOEs), certified by CBTUI the
supporting documentation would be retained by CBTU and would be available for
review during project supervision.   Retroactive financing amounting to no more
than US$10.0 million would be permitted for project related expenditures incurred
after September 1, 1991.
3.21 During loan negotiations, it was confirmed that CBTU has made provisions
for: (a) counterpart funds required for project expenditures according to work
and procurement schedules; (b) continuing to retain independent auditors,
acceptable to the Bank, to audit its accounts, including the projects accounts,
in accordance with appropriate auditing principles; (c) forwarding the annual
audit reports to the Bank not later than four months after the end of the fiscal
year; and that (d) the Special Account would be audited by independent auditors
acceptable to the Bank.

(c)     Financial Performance Targets:   they will be the basis for the
monitoring of the financial performance of the project. CBTU's
financial performance and that of CBTU-SP, would gradually improve
with the project as a result of the proposed physical and operating
improvements, tariff adjustments and increased passenger traffic.
Farebox revenues will increase by 361% in CBTU-SP from 1990 to 1996.
During the five-year project implementation period, the working ratios
(the ratio of working costs divided by farebox revenues plus other
operating revenues) of CBTU-SP are projected to decline from 4.23 in
1990 to 1.0 in 1996. Even though traffic is expected to increase by
53% in CBTU-SP, operating subsidy payments will gradually decrease
through the efficiency improvements. Operating subsidy reductions per
paying passenger in the MR of SP are more dramatic, declining from
US$0.30 to US$0.03 in CBTU-SP. Since salaries and wages constitute
more than 50% of total working costs, additional improvements might
occur from a successful implementation of manpower studies financed
under the Project.  The capital subsidy (new investments, ioe the
World  Bank  project,  plus  debt  service)  during  the  project
iraplementation period will be a Federal obligation and will not impact
the CBTU-SP from 1992 to 1996.
Financial performance targets as shown below (Table 3.4) will also be agreed
to during loan negotiations. In view of the tariffs presently charged by the
FEPASA tletropolitan train, which are 3 times those of CBTU in the same
metropolitan region, it is expected that a rehabilitated CBTU-SP will attract the
demand required to achieve the proposed financial targets.  In addition, the
project calls for several changes in the maintenance organization and in the
materials management area.   Those changes are expected to cut costs and
contribute to the achievement of the proposed working ratio targets.   Pro-forma
income statements, balance sheets and sources and applications of funds for CBTU-
SP after the rehabilitation are shown in Annex 5B.

J.  id-Term Proiect Review
3.23 A Mid-term Project Review: (tentatively recommended for June 1993) will take
place to review preagreed institutional, operational and financial performance
indicators, and compliance with covenants (including tariffs, subsidies, progress
on studies, etc) of the Loan and Project Agreements, to assess project
performance and progress on decentralization and to propose corrective measures,
if any. Particular importance will be given, during the mid-term review, to the
agreed working ratios and the status of the studies and action plans required
to ensure a smooth Operational Transfer.
K. Proiect Economic Evaluation Summary
3.24 As this project was prepared jointly with a possible project for CBTU-RJ,
the economic evaluation of the project was done for CBTU-RJ and CBTU-SP as a
whole and separately. The evaluation was also undertaken for each of the main
corridors in both CBTU-JR and CBTU-SP so as to determine whether any of the
corridors should be dropped from the project. Some assumptions had to be made
in the corridor analysis insofar as rolling stock is concerned, in order to
artificially assign it to each corridor. In addition to the above evaluation,
an analysis of incremental economic rate of return of the electrification of
4.51on of track in CBTU-RJ was also undertaken. The "overall" and "by corridor"
ERR's for the base case are shown in Table 3.5. The evaluation is conservative
and the results show that the S. Paulo rehabilitation project is economically
viable.
