3.25 Substantial quantifiable and non-quantifiable benefits are expected from
the provision of higher capacity and safer operation by CBTU, namely, operating
cost savings, time savings for the users, reduction of road congestion, less
accidents and fatalities and employment generation through better spatial
integration of markets. There will also be a contribution to the improvement of
air quality and noise pollution, since a great number of riders will switch from
diesel operated buses to the electrified railways which are cleaner and less
noisy. Finally, poverty alleviation will also result from improved accessibility
to employment.  The benefits quantified in the economic evaluation were: (a)
direct benefits such as the reduction of trip cost and the value of time savings,
reduction of the road maintenance cost due to lesser buses, reduction of the
operating costs associated with the traffic control of buses; (b) indirect
benefits such as the reduction of congestion due to lesser buses, reduction of
road accidents and the reduction of environmental pollution.   The indirect
benefits were not included in the base cases evaluated.  They were only used for
sensitivity analysis purposes. The costs quantified in the economic analysis
were: a) the long-run marginal costs of the CBTU-RJ and CBTU-SP operation before
and after the project, which were estimated using standard costs to reflect less
erratic maintenance policies, and b) the economic investment costs of the
program.
3.26 The ERR for the base case of CBTU-SP was 46%. The sensitivity analysis on
traffic forecasts, increased investment costs, deferral of benefits to the fourth
year, etc., produced acceptable results which ranged from 23% (case in which
benefits are deferred to the fourth year and investments cost 10% higher) to 60%
(case in which the benefits faom externalities are added).  A more detailed
description of the economic evaluation is given in Annex SA.
IV. DECENTRALIZATION. INSTITUTIONAL AND FINIIANCIAL POLICY RFORMS
4.1 The transfer of CBTU-SP subdivision from the GOB to the State of Sao Paulo
has been agreed, and formalized through the execution of an Agreement of Intent
(Convenio Ba9ico) on August 30, 1991.



- 26 -
A. Decentralization
4,2  Transitional Phase to Decentralization:   As the successful transfer for
decentralization will be subject to detailed negotiations, and will involve some
political considerations in addition to financial and technical issues, the
detailed steps and transition to decentralization are viewed as a dynamic process
involving the full and transparent participation of all levels of Government.
In order to underpin this process, joint and parallel strategic studies,
administrative and legal actions, organizational changes and key political
decisions would have to be undertaker. in a logical and sequential manner, and
synchronized with the timetable for processing and executing the proposed Bank
Loan and eventually for transfer of the system to SP not later than the target
date of December 31, 1993 (see Charts 1-3).
(a)     General Coordination and Completion of Strategic Studies: immediately
following signature of the Convenio Basico, the General Coordination
Committee (CG) to manage and guide the decentralization process. The
CG will  incorporate  a number  of  task  forces.    The  CG,  with
representatives from the CBTU and SP will report to the Conselho
Diretor through the Representantes Executivos (see Chart 2). The task
forces will work on the strategic studies and Actions Plans (para.
3.4(i)) outlined in the Convenio Basico.   A draft outline of the
detailed terms of reference and an agreed work program, prepared by
CBTU in collaboration with SP respectively, for the task forces on
decentralization (as specified in Clause II, para 6. of the Convenio
Basico) will be submitted to the Bank prior to Negotiations. The
output of these studies will determine the additional contractual
responsibilities of all signatories to the Convenio Basico (in
addition to the provisions under the Loan and Project Agreement).
These will be formalized through the execution of Supplementary
Agreements (Termos Additivos) to the Convenio Basico.
(b)     Transfer to Public Corporations:   in accordance with the Federal
Government's policy for modernization of the economy, and SP's long-
standing tradition for efficiency, the CBTU-SP will be transferred to
a Public Corporation (Sociedade Anonima de Economia Mista). Assurances
will be sought at Negotiations that, not later than June 30, 1993, SP
would set up such Public Corporation, under an independent Board and
management autonomy, and thereafter, not later than December 31, 1993,
the CBTU-SP subdivision will be fully transferred to the Corporation.
The articles of Incorporation would include for, among other things,
that (i) the Corporation will be managed by professionally trained
Chief Executive Officern (CEOs); (ii) the management will adhere to
specific operating financial and performance targets set forth in
annual contract plans; (iii) management will abide by agreed personnel
and administrative policies; and (iv) generally follow sound
commercial practices. Draft Articles for r:he Corporation will be
reviewed by the Bank.
4.3   imetable and Phasing for Decentralization.  The project has been prepared
to take into account the Federal government's objective to complete the legal and
institutional transfer (DECENTRALIZATION) of the CBTU-SP by the target date of
December 31, 1993, although rehabilitation will continue to 1996. However, since



- 27 -
complete decentralization of assets require legislative amendments for which
-approval dates cannot be set with certainty, it was agreed:
a) to introduce an intermediate step herein called "Operational Transfer"
which would permit the transfer to proceed as planned not later than
December 31, 1993, pending approval of legislation to permit the "Ownership
Transfer" of the CBTU-SP system to take place as a second stage.  Both
terms are defined in the legal documents (Section 1.02 (t) and 1.02 (u) of
the LA) as follows;
i) " Operational Transfer" means the effective transfer to the SP
Entity of all management and operational and responsibility and
accountability for the operation and administration of the CBTU-SP
System.
ii) "Ownership Transfer" means the transfer of ownership of the CBTU-
SP System to the SP Entity including the enactment and execution of
all legal, contractual and administrative acts and documents required
under the legislation of the Borrower and the State for these
purposes*
b)   It was agreed to include a specific dated covenant (Section 3.06) in
the Loan Agreement concerning the submission to the Federal Congress on an
amendment to a Bill of Law or of a Bill of Law that would permit the
conclusion of the decentralization process.
c)   The terms of the operational transfer will include a validity period
of 50 years which shall be terminated, after appropriate legal
authorizations are given and put into effect, by the effective transfer of
CBTU-SP System to the SP Entity. The operational transfer will provide the
SP Entity with all the rights and obligations required from the
administration of the system. The terms and conditions of the Operational
Transfer will be set out in a contract to be signed between CBTU and the
SP Entity.
4.4  Contractual Arrangements:   In order to ensure a smooth transition to
decentralization, (the formal legal transfer and start of operations by the SP
Operating Corporation (see para 4.2(a)), and to support its subsequent
consolidation, the following legal arrangements will apply: (See Chart 4.1/Annex
4.c)
With the Banks
(a) Bank and GOB    (Borrower)               Loan Agreement
(b) Bank and CBTU   (Executing Agency)        Project Agreement
(c) Bank and SP      (Operating Corporati6n) Project Agreement
Between Governments and agencies:
(d) GOB and SP               (Convenio Basico and Termos Additivos)
(e) CBTU and SP Entity       (Operational Transfer Contract)
B. Modal Integration
4.5 Bank support for this project was based on early agreement that
institutionalizing a policy of multimodal integration is a "sine qua non" for



- 28 -
this project with the objective of: (a) improving multi-year investment planning
and physical integration of different services, introducing integrated tariff and
operational policies, and strengthening of the regulatory framework for managing
public transport; (b) strengthening the institutional framework and management
of each mode in a parallel and consistent basis to minimize cross-inefficienciesI
and (c) defining a clear role and responsibility for the private sector in the
provision of transport services and related infrastructure in the MRs.
4.6 The project will finance selected studies, which will recommend immediate
actions to be implemented by the state and municipalities of the MR, and form the
basis to facilitate urther reforms in the sector, as follows: (a) an updating
of the existing Master Transportation Plan prepared for the Municipality of SP
(MSP) and the SP Metropolitan Region (SPMR) and a Route Rationalization Study for
the SPMR; (b) a Management Study focussing on policy and an institutional
development action plan of the MSP-owned bus company (CMTC); (c) an operations
and Management Study cf the FEPASA Metropolitan Train to define alternative
options for its future operations, including its integration with the
decentralized CBTU-SP; (d) a study to review the Regulatory framework within
which "concessions" and "permissions" are granted in MSP and SPMR in order to
facilitate the implementation of recommendations of the Route Rationalization
Studies, and (e) a multi-modal tariff integration study.
4.7   A State Secretary of Metropolitan Transport to establish institutional
arrangements on a permanent basis, to plan, coordinate and set priorities for
integrated urban transport in the SPMR was created by SP by Law No. 7.450 on July
16, 1991 and regulatios setting out its organization were established by Decree
No. 34.184 dated Noveember 18, 1991. This was a encouraging first step towards
enhancement of urban transport coordination in the SPMR, one of the main
objectives of the project. At negotiations agreement was reached on (a) the CBTU
and SP will undertake to ensure completion of selected studies before the Mid-
Term Project Review, so that an Action Plan on the agreed recommendations can be
implemented; and b) that, not later than 12 months from the date of signing of
the Loan Agreement, the State of Sao Paulo will present a plan (financial,
technical and institutional) and a timetable, satisfactory to the Bank, for the
transfer, integration into the SP corporation, and consolidation of the
metropolitan train system (FEPASA).
C. Financial Policy
4.8  Poverty,  Affordability  and Subsidies:    The  growing crisis  in urban
transportation in 1razil, especially in all the MRs demands a close look at
alternatives for financing services in a more predictable and sustainable way
while, at the same time, targeting subsidies more directly to low-income
ridership.   The various initiatives already underway within CBTU to improve
efficiency (see paras. 2.8-2.9) through tariff increases, quality and service
capacity and trip reliability, as well as efforts focused on reduced operating
costs, are planned to continue.
4.9 The ridership of the CBTU is predominantly of very low income - about 58Z
in SP earn less than 3 minimum wages and about 78X earn less than 5 minimum
salaries (only the very desperate will travel under the appalling conditions
today). The most recent consistent data on household expenses and income (IBGE,



-29 -
Family Budget Survey, 1987/88) shows that basic expenses!' (except urban
transportation), for families declaring revenues equal or less than two minimum
wages (MW), exceed average family income. If urban transportation (UT) expenses
are added to basic expenses, almost all household income is used up, even for
those earning between 2 and 5 MWs (Annex 4B).
4.10   Given the scarcity of reliable aggregate data on household expenses
required to estimate the share of UT expenses incurred by a low income household
as a X of household income, it was necessary to perform a simulation exercise
based on current UT fares and MW, etc. (see Annex 4B). This simulation shows
that UT expenses (based on a home to work ride which includes the use of bus plus
rail), in 1991, account for at least 61% of total hosehold income. UT expenses
plus basic expenses as a share of household income, went up in comparison to
1987/88.   For household earning up to 2 MW, those figures for SP are 161%
(1987/88) and 211% (1991)4I. Bus fares' real growth in the period accounts for
most of that increase.
4.11 The same exercise was done to evaluate the impact on low-income families'
budget of the proposed rail rates after CBTU decentralization in SPMR (US$0.33).
By the time the project is completed (1996) and even assuming a sizeable minimum
wage increase (MW assumed at US$100 in 1991 constant dollars), the costs of urban
transportation for poor households in terms of the consumption of basic goods and
services is going to rise.   It is estimated that in 1995 UT costs (bus-rail
combined trips) will correspond to about 66% of household income in SP (61% in
1991) for households with incomes up to 2 MW. It is therefore clear that while
the program envisages a substantial fare increase (pegged to the urban bus fares
in each capital city) the average income level of CBTU's riders present severe
affordability constraints for full cost recovery, especially for rail-based
travel.
4.12 To make UT affordable to the poor it is necessary to strengthen and expand
the eligibility to the "vale-transporte" program to low income residents which
are not formally employed, to substantially promote integrated (bus-rail, subway-
rail) fares, which could be lower than the combined fare assumed in Annex 4B, and
to increase the overall level of service provided to the users. This will imply
lower travel times due to higher frequencies and operating speeds bringing down
therefore the overall generalized transport cost to the user thus alleviating the
burden that urban transportation imposes on poor households.
4.13 Unlike rail fares, bus fares do not contribute to road maintenance which
is the responsibility of local Government and is financed out of general
revenues. Therefore, even after a successful cost reduction program, rail based
operating deficits reflect (i) a small percentage for the financing of
maintenance costs for fixed infrastructure and (ii) a directly targeted subsidy
to help alleviate poverty. In order to maintain the future financial health of
the SP Entity after decentralization, and to ensure adequate and timely
maintenance and safety of the system, it will be necessary to ensure a reliable
and predictable source of financing to meet these "normalization" payments.
3/ Food, housing, clothing, health care and education.
4/ Figures higher than 100% are both due to understatement of monetary earnings and to the
relatively higher share of non-monetary earnings in those households.



_ 30 -
4.14 Impact of Decentralization on Local Finances. The financial situation of
both the states of SP and RJ has been uneven in recent years.   Successive
macroeconomic stabilization plans, high inflation rates, and changes in the
legislation concerning tax collection and indebtedness have led to a somciwhat
erratic behavior of both revenues and expenditures.   Until 1988, the underlying
trend was one of deteriorating financial performance and increasing recourse to
borrowing.   In 1989 a generalized improvement was registered, mostly as a
consequence of the implementation of the new constitution which has, inter-alia,
enlarged the tax basis and the revenue share of sub-national governments. In
1990, the situation deteriorated again, as local tax revenues were negatively
affected by the serious economic recession (Brazil's GDP fell 4.5Z in real
terms).
4.15 The evaluation of the capacity of the local governments to assume the
financial responsibilities of the decentralized sub-divisions was carried out
taking into account past and future financial performance and the projected needs
for government financing to cover the difference between working expenses and
passenger generated revenues (normalizatiowu costs). The financial situation of
the federative governments depends largely on the fiscal decentralization
included in the 1988 Constitution. While decentralization of expenditures is
still awaiting complementary legislation, the decentralization of revenues is
already underway and will be complete in 1993. There are no official estimates
of the net impact the reform will have on local (or central) governments.
Preliminary estimates indicate that in relation to 1987-88, states' revenue level
may increase as much as 10-15%; and municipalities revenue by 30-35%.0'
Expenditure responsibilities will increase as well, but the net effect is still
uncertain. For the purposes of this project, one has assumed conservatively that
any increase in local revenues would not be totally offset by corresponding
increase in expenditures, so as to avoid further built-up of arreare or
outstanding debt. In addition, one assumed that economic activity in 1992 would
recover and that tax revenues would recover to the level of 1989.
4.16 In the case of Sao Paulo (SP), both financial performance and the size of
the normalization payments suggest that the state can meet the needs of the new
Corporation. On the one hand, the fiscal performance of Sao Paulo in the last
five years has been reasonably good: the state has been able to generate a small
but constant current surplus with which it financed about one-third of its
investment expenditures.-   On the other hand, the size of the normalization
requirements is relatively small and the State of Sao Paulo has met on a timely
basis all contracted subsidies agreed with both FEPASA and the Sao Paulo Metro.
The annual amount the state would need to finance is estimated at $65 million
which compared with the $1900 million annually spent by the state in
transportation activities is relatively modest. That is, the additional cost of
decentralization would represent roughly 3% of the state budget allocated to
transport in the last five years. Taking into account the potential savings
obtained from other transport modes as the institutional capacity of the sector
is strengthened (i.e. with the creation of the state transport authority) and the
5/ For more details see Anwar Shah, The New Fiscal Federalism in Brazil, World Bank Discussion
paper, No, 124. 1991.
6/ The remaining was financed with borrowed revenues and that has led to an accumulation of debt
(declared or undeclared) and of debt service. In 1990, debt service represented 30% of the state
revenues.



- 31 -
possibility of additional sources of tariff revenues, once the tariff study is
complete, the amount under discussion is likely to be financed on a timely basis.
4.17 Review of Financing Hechanisms. In conclusion, it was agreed that efforts
should be focused on seeking a solution(s) that (a) would generate sufficient
resources to maintain a stable and predictable funding for urban transport
investments; (b) mechanisms be established to generate adequate operating cross-
subsidies from within transport related taxes, user charges, etc., to provide
adequate support for normalization (operating deficits) transters; and (c) CBTU
would contract with a credible institution (such as IPEA) to provide a structured
leadership to a Tariff and Financial Policy study, to conduct discussions at the
federal, state and local levels, and to help in drafting the appropriate
legislation to implement such policy reforms at the national level.
4.18 In the meantime, agreement was reached at negotiations so that (a) During
the project disbursement period, the GOB will meet the project-related cost of
capital investments plus debt service (interest and amortization) of the CBTU-SP;
(b) during the period prior to Operational Transfer , GOB will cover the differ-
ence between the working costs and operating revenues of each sub-division; (c)
and that after the Operational Transfer is effected the GOB operating subsidy
(shortfall between operating revenues and working expenses) to the SP Entity will
be discontinued.
V. ENVIRONM4ENTAL IMPACT OF THE PROJECT
5.1 Since the main objective of the proposed project is to increase the capacity
offered by the rail suburban system, it is expected that a substantial percentage
of the passengers now carried by bus will return to their original mode of
transportation, that is, rail. This is especially true for passengers who live
within walking distance from the stations, who switched to bus because, in the
last five years, the capacity and safety offered by the trains decreased
considerably. However, it is not so obvious for those passengers who are not
within walking distance. Nevertheless, theoretically it is estimated that by
project completion about a thousand (1,000) less buses/day will be required in
the streets of the Sao Paulo metropolitan area, because over 320,000 pass/day are
conservatively expected to switch back to rail. The main positive environmental
impacts expected stem from this reduction in bus services, given their present
negative impact on the air quality, noise levels, traffic congestion, and road
accidents. Other positive impacts as discussed below, include the effect of the
rehabilitation of the rail system on passenger safety at the stations, on 'the
land use around the right of way and the decrease of fatal accidents resulting
from lack of fencing of the right-of-way.
5.2 The impact on air quality is due to a reduction of emission by the buses
which will be removed from circulation, or which will have their route-km.
shortened because, they will be operating feeder services for the rail system
rather than duplicating their routes. The impact on air quality was estimated
at 6.3 million US$/year in S. Paulo. The impact on noise levels is also due to
the removal of buses, especially in Sao Paulo, where the older vehicles
predominantly owned by the public sector are noisy and when bunched together,
cause very high db levels, which hardly allow people to chat at normal voice
levels on the street. This impact was quantified at 1.3 million US$/year in S.



- 32 -
Paulo.   The impact on traffic congestion was also estimated at 8.4 million
US$/year in S. Paulo and is mainly due to the shorter waiting time for commuters
using their automobiles and buses and also due to the savings in fuel consumption
since the vehicles consume more when they are idle. The effect on road accidents
is based on statistics which suggest that, in the metropolitan region, the number
of bus accidents is a function of the number of buses on the road. It is
estimated that the savings due to lesser accidents are 1.7 million US$/year in
S. Paulo. In addition, the fencing of the right-of-way is expected to decrease
the number of fatal accidents due to people who cross the tracks. Even if the
benefits estimated were cut in half because the bus oporator. would, in .cGe
cases, continue to run the buses at lower load factors, the project would
contribute positively to the environment.   Details are given in Annex Sc.
Benefits which were not quantified are those related to the improved living
environment which will result from aafer and cleaner stations, crossovers from
the sidewalks to the stations, space for queuing up at the wickets, ramps for the
handicapped and, in some cases, modal integration with taxis, buses and private
automobiles. Other non-quantified benefits are those related to the structuring
effect of a revamped rail system which will attract housing in the surrounding
areas. Furthermore, real estate development on the air space above the stations
and on land owned by CBTU is also expected. Other non-quantified benefits relate
to the lesser stress which the road system and congestion will impart on the road
users and the better ambience expected in trains where, at present, people are
carried as cattle. Shorter trip times and faster home-to-work trips will also
have a positive effect on the rail users, welfare.
5.3 The proposed physical rehabilitati in of the system is not expected to have
negative impacts.  Most materials used will not affect the environment, the
contractors will be responsible for proper disposal of old materials, the
earthworks are mainly in CBTU's existing right-of-way and there is no
expropriation.   Wooden ties will only be procured from contractors who are
allowed by their national agencies to sell them and, wherever feasible, concrete
ties will be used.  The visual impact of the stations will be enhanced with
appropriate architectural designs which will blend with the environment and the
same will be done with the overhead catenary, wherever possible. In short, the
environmental impact of the project is expected to be positive. Assurances will
be sought that the project will be executed, and the system subsequently operated
in accordance with good environmental practices.
VI. PROJECT RISKS. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Project Risks
6.1 The main risks involved are the dynamics and consequent delays in the
dialogue between the Federal and State Governments, the political reluctance to
raising tariffs which will allow each sub-division to reach the targeted working
ratios, the possible delays in the procurement process, and the timely
availability of counterpart funds. Negotiations between the GOB and SP have
progressed very well and the same is true for the negotiations between the SP and
MSP  for modal coordination.   All parties have  agreed  on the thrust  of
decentralization and the risks are more associated with its phasing and term of
its transfer. The tariff issue will remain a major risk although CBTU has been
increasing tariffs, because Governments may be tempted to freeze tariffs for long
periods of time, thereby eroding revenues.   The contract plan between the



- 33 -
operating entity and the Government will contain provisions to protect the CBTU-
SP systems from these imposed freezes. The delays in the procurement process
will be minimized by requiring the borrower to produce the major tender documents
by Board Presentation. The timely availability of counterpart funds will be
eddressed by seeking assurances that they are already included in the Federal
Government FY92 provisions for CBTU and will be included for every year
thereafter. The proposed Mid-term Review (para. 3.22) will also monitor this
condition.
Agreements Reached
6.2   The following assurances have been obtained:
A. Project Management:
a)     that the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) will be structured and
adequately staffed and otherwise supported in the implementation of
its responsibility under the project (para. 3.9);
b)     that  Procurement will  be  carried  out  in  accordance with  Bank
guidelines, including (i) the use of standard bidding documents for
the procurement of goods and works, under LCB and ICB procedures; (ii)
use of Bank's guidelines for the selection of consultants; and (iii)
the procurement limits (para. 3.17);
c)     that Special Accounts in BACEN and in a Commercial Bank will be opened
and maintained (para. 3.20);
d)     that Special Accounts and Statements of Expenditures (SOEs) (i) will
be audited according to procedures, and by independent auditors,
acceptable to the Bank, and (ii) audits will be submitted within four
months of year-end (para. 3.21);
e)     that (i) within ninety days of loan signing a Project Management
Information System (PMIS) satisfactory to the Bank be installed; (ii)
preagreed  institutional,   physical,   operational  and  financial
performance targets to be included in quarterly and annual reports to
the Bank; and (iii) an independent project monitoring consultant,
reporting to the President of CBTU, be appointed to maintain a
continuing performance audit of the project, and recommend corrective
actions where required (para. 3.10-3.11);
f)     that the project will be executed,  and the system subsequently
operated in accordance with good environmental practices ( set forth
in a Side Letter to the Loan Agreement) (para. 5.3);
B. Decentralitation and Operational Efficiency:
g)     that the Operational Transfer will be completed by the target date of
December 31, 1993, or when 60Z of the loan funds have been disbursed,
whichever date is earlier, in accordance with a phased program
previously agreed with the Bank (para. 4.3);

to  the  Borrower  and  the  Bank,  to  effect  the  Operational

Borrower shall submit   to the Borrower's Congress a bill of law, in
form and substance satisfactory to the Bank, authorizing the Borrower
to take all action required to effect the Ownership Transfer (para.
4.3 (b)).
j)     that not later than June 30,  1993,  SP establish a juridically

to the Bank , (the Contract Plans) including specific  operating,
financial and performance targets for the following fiscal year and
the financial contributions and other actions required from the State
to enable the SP Entity to attain such targets. (para. 4.2(b));
k)     that CBTU up to December 31, 1993, will take all necessary actions to:
(i) carry out the Project in accordance with the Implementation
Program set forth in tke Schedule to the CBTU Project Agreement; and
(ii) achieve agreed basic physical, operational and financial targets.
(para. 3.22(a)-(c));

include a specific program of tariff increases and a set of

operating performance targets to maintain efficiency (para. 3.22);
C. Financial Policy and Modal Integration2

ANNEX 1
Page 1 of 8
BRAZIL
METROPOLITAN TRANSPORT DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
BANK ASSISTANCE TO THE URBAN TRANSPORT SECTOR IN BRAZIL
1.     This Annex outlines the Bank's past support for the Brazilian Urban
Transport Sector and the experience gained.   The lessons learned can be
summarized ass (a) the coordination between the three levels of government in
urban transport cannot be assumed, and must be dealt with very carefully at the
project preparation level, and reviewed in detail during the mid-term project
review; (b) policy for the sector must be strengthened to minimize distortions
resulting from inefficient physical and financial coordination between modes and
to promote multimodal integration; (c) the tariff levels should allow for
significant cost recovery of working costs and must be complemented by financing
mechanisms which cover the shortfall; (d) demand levels forecast by the borrowers
should be carefully scrutinized; and (e) more attention must be given to
maintenance management on infrastructure.
A. FIRST URBAN TRANSPORT PROJECT (LOAN 1563-BR, US$88 MILLION, M&Y 1978)
2.    Project Objectives:  (a) to finance investments that would improve urban
transport services, with emphasis on the provision of public transport,
especially to the poor; (b) to promote and support the development and
implementation of appropriate urban transport policies; and (c) to strengthen
municipal, state, and federal capacity to prepare, appraise, and execute sound
urban transport projects.
3.     Project Cont,nt:   The project was broad in scope, and included: (a)
traffic engineering and management measures in central urban areas and at
critical points, the introduction of exclusive bus lanes and segregated busways,
widening of critical links, or construction of missing links in the road network,
reorganization or construction of bus terminals, paving of bus routes in low-
income areas, and training, technical a6sistance and institution strengthening
to improve the delivery of transport services. Project beneficiaries included
the five MRs (Belo Horizonte, Curitiba, Porto Alegre, Recife and Salvador). Some
nine different agencies were involved in implementation and overall coordination
was the responsibility of EBTU.
4.     Implementation Experlince: The project experienced various delays due to:
(a) lack of institutional support to the cities from EBTU, and Government changes
in 1979 which led to otaff changes in the various implementing agencies; (b) the
preparation of final engineering designs and tendering; (c) land acquisition; (d)
availability of counterpart funds; (e) local level coordination, and (f) quality
control of the works. The closing date for the project was extended twice,
resulting in an implementation period from three to five years and nine months.
This suggests that the appraisal schedule estimate was overly optimistic but
performance was still substantially shorter than both worldwide and LAC region
profiles which were above eight years. The only specific covenant, other than
the standard conditions, was not met. The five municipalities were required to
take the necessary steps to revise the valuation for tax purposes of properties



- 48 -
ANNEX 1
Page 2 of 8
whose values have been affected by the project.  In the early stages of the
project, EBTU staff were less forceful than expected in solving local problems
and bringing difficulties to the attention of decision-makers.   It must be
recognized, however, that EBTU was in its formative stages. This was a first
project for them and there was a learning process involved. As part of this
process, and in order to achieve institutional improvement, EBTU prepared several
operational manuals to assist in the appraisal and supervision processes. These
manuals and the experience gained by EBTU (and the Bank) provided the basis for
the development of procedures applied in thie Third Urban Transport Project
(1981), which did much to improve the quality and efficiincy of EBTU's
operations.
5.     Results:  The project was timely and helped to orientate the Government's
growing concern about urban transport problems.   The support given to the
institutional development of EBTU, following its creation in 1976, improved
federal level urban transport planning. The project was successful in providing
improved urban transport services, particularly public transport to serve the
poor. The OED concludes that "the project was successful in implementing some
of the most imaginative and radical buis priority measures carried out anywhere
in the world. These experiences have relevance for Bank-financed urban transport
projects worldwide". The bus priority measures included the development of the
bus corridor concept.   Bus corridors were designed to accommodate passenger
demand levels which were difficult to satisfy by conventional bus services.
Schemes involved segregated busways introduced into the existing road system,
facilities for passengers and pedestrians, bus system reorganization (usually
trunk/feeder), traffic management measures and integrated bus fare systems. The
project was also generally successful in providing institutional oupport at the
MR level where there has been, a significant improvement in the capacity of the
agencies to tackle urban transport iseues; the success was most particularly
noted in those Mrs where future urban transport investments existed, not
necessarily financed by the Bank, which provided the scope for continued
institutional support. Finally, the studies and institutional strengthening,
financed under the project, coupled with the successful implementation of the
physical components, have led to policy changes affecting urban transport in
Brazil as whlole and, in particular, lead to increased emphasis on public bus
transport and greater attention to cost-effectivo policies to traffic management.
6.     Sustainability:   Institutional, technical and financial resources are
required to sustain the types of investments included in the First Urban
Transport Project.   Although there were some institutional and technical
weaknesses, the project clearly provided institutional oupport to strengthen EBTU
and the local agencies. In the case of EBTU, this support was continued In the
Third  Urban  Transport  Project.    The  major  constraint  with  respect  to
sustainability hlas been the lack of provision for cost recovery, or other funds
to cover maintenanice of the physical schemes Implemented under the project. The
failure to make provision for maintenance of the Investments is seen in
practically all the city projects.
7.    HEowever, the project had a substantial institutional impact overall. It
assisted EBTU at a critical and formative otago of its development, by providing
strong techntiical and financial support, which enabled it to play a catalytic role
in transport improvements in urban areas. EBTU subsequently took increaningly
more responoibility in tho approicll anid supervioion of Bnnk-finanecd urban
transport projects, a procesa whtich began withl the five MRs included In the Firot



- 49 -
ANNER I
Page 3 of 8
Urban Transport Project, and which culminated with full responsibility for
appraisal and supervision of eleven out of the fourteen urban centers included
in the Third Urban Traneport Project.
8.     The institutional support resulting from the technical assi&tance and
studies, coupled with the experience gained generally from the successful
itplementation of the physical components included in the First Urban Transport
Project, have led to urban transport policy changes which affect Brazil as a
whole; these include more emphasis on traffic management, increased emphasis on
the provision of public bus transport, and the creation of coordinating bodies
at the local level to implement urban transport policy.
B. SECOND URBAN TRANSPORT (PORTO ALEGRE) PROJECT (LOAN 1839-BR, US$S59
MILLION, JUNE 1980)
9.    Project Objectives:  (a) to establish a new company to own, manage, and
operate a suburban rail mass transit system on a commercial basis without
operating subsidies; (b) to achieve a significantly more efficient modal
distribution of traffic and use of all transport facilities in the metropolitan
region's North-South corridor; and (c) to promote the development of selecaed
employment growth poles In the North-South corridor near urban railway stations.
10.    Project. Content:  The project consisted of the construction of a surface
suburban rail mass transit system usiug electric unit trains between the central
business area In Porto Alegre and the municipality of Sapucaia, 26.7 km to the
North; the establishment of a new company to own, manage and operate this
services and a complementary program of policies and studies.
I1.    Implementation Experiences Despite some serious problems of procurement,
passenger services started in March 1985, one year after the origineally planned
sterting date. The railway company, TRENSURB, had successfully been constituted
and during the course of project implementation had changed its nature from a
construction organization to a railway operating company. The closing date was
extended once by one year to December 31, 1986.
12.    Reaultas   The Project Completion Report sums up well the principal
lessonst
"(A) It Is not sufficient that the railway service has been successfully
implemented.   The success of the project depends upon the service
attracting sufficient passengers to make the investments economically
viable and the railway profitable. This will be highly contingent upon
the resumption of economic growth In Porto Alcgro and In the evolution of
tho fares for the users of alternative transport modes. In addition, the
attractiveness of the system would be enhanced by the Implementation of
the recoemendations of the studioe carried out under the project (bus
integration, car parking restrictions, etc.);
(b)     Ohe major lesson to be learned from thie project is that, In
cases In which there is a need for substantial policy decisions and the
implementation of measures Involving different Government agencies
subject  to  conflicting  pressures,   their  commitment  should  be
realletically assessed duritng project preparation. The steps to eneare



- 50 -
ANNEX 1
Page 4 of 8
that these policies are implemented on time should be carefully
identified at the same time. In the particular case of the Second Urban
Transport Project, a more detailed action program including specific
policy implementation targets and appropriate measures for its
enforcement should have been agreed upon during appraisal; and
(c)     Regarding the original objective of TRENSURB covering its working
costs, the appraisal was unrealistic to explect that this would take place
during the initial operation period.   Only after the system   hae
demonstrated its reliability to the usero, will the application of a
revenue maximizing policy be feasible. In the meantime, TRENSURB should
roview its staff needs in order to streamline costs and carry out a
tariff study to determine the optimal user charges.   The Bank should
closely follow-up the implementation of these tasks in the context of its
ongoing and future involvement in the sector and continue to impress upon
the Government the benefits to be gained from a financially healthy
public enterprise as well as the existencet of tools much more effective
than transport subsidies to achieve income distribution objectives."
C.  THIRD URBAN TRANSPORT PROJECT (LOAN 1965-BR, US$90 MILLION, MARCH 1981)
13.    Project Objectives:  (a) to improve the quality and efficiency of EBTU's
operations through changes in its structure, procedures, and staff allocation;
(b) to improve transport infrastructure and operations in 14 medium-sized cities
and conurbations (AGLURB); and (c) to pave bus routes in low-income areas in
medium-sized cities, state capitals, and metropolitan regions (PROPAV).
14.    Project Content:  The project consists of three subprojects:   (a) the
institutional subproject includes EBTU's reorganization, training, technical
assistance and studies; (b) the AGLURB subproject consists of the establishment
and operation of an urban transport management unit in each of the 14 cities and
various components in low-cost improvements to existing urban transport
infrastructure with et-phasis on public transport operations and improvements of
planning and operation of urban transport infrastructure and services at the
local level; and (c) the PROPAV subproject includes the paving of about 850 km
of bus routes in low-income areas.
15.    ImplementstionExperience: Project implementation delays were experienced
as a result of numerous political changes, limited counterpart funds, the
transfer of EBTU to the MDU from MT and, soon thereafter, the Sarney Government's
Ecotomic Package, which delayed the signing of the 1986 Convenios by about six
montls. The project was amended twice, which included for the addition of four
cities to the AGLURB subproject and of 350 km of bus routes in low-income areas
to the PROPAV subproject, and resulted also in an extension in the implementation
period from four years and three months to five years and nine monthe. EBTU was
In compliance with .1l loan covenants.
16.    Resultst  The AGLURB subproject has done much to create a reallitic and
effective transport planning environment in Brazilian medium-sized conurbations.
The design of the physical componento drew heavily on the successful experience
from the First Urban Transport Project. Emphasis of the project waO placed on
cost-effective, traffic management based schemes in which assistance to public
transport (buses) has been the primary objective tn the majority of components.



-- 51 -
Page 5 of 8
The institutional development of the AGLURB areas was achieved through the
establishment  of  management  units  specifically  to  coordinate  project
implementation. Transport companies were formed to coordinate transport planning
and operation. Retention of the management units would depend on decisions by
individual cities but selected personnel would be transferred from the management
units to the transport companies.   At the time of project completion, the
implementation delays arising from the political, financial, and organizational
factors meant that comprehensive ex post evaluations were not yet available. As
with the First Urban Transport Project, there is a risk that the institutional
gains may not be sustained unless a flow of funds is made available to AGLURB
cities to continue purposeful transport planning.
17.    The PROPAV program was innovative and was a significant step in EBTU's
development.   PROPAV was essentially a subsectoral component and EBTU was
responsible for all aspects of the program. The program utilized local materials
and labor-intensive construction methods to the maximum practical extent and
thus, the costs were initially overestimated. This permitted a greatcr than
targeted program to be undertaken and to date all 850km of paving have been
implemented. The PROPAV program is regarded as a highly succe6sful program. To
an extent, the success of the program is demonstrated by the fact that similar
paving programs have been undertaken in other countries in the region.
18.    The institutional strengthening comprised two main aspectst (a) EBTU
reorganization;  and (b) training and studies.   The reorgani2ation of EBTU
proposed under the Third Project was completed in 1984.   In general, the
reorganization was judged to have increased EBTU's effectiveness. However, with
the changing demands arising from federal government policy on decentralization
and from the needs of the Fourth Urban Transport Project, additional changes were
assessed as necessary.
19.    The training program comprised a conventional approach (of courses,
seminars, etc.) to transport planners and an imaginative training program for bus
operators and personnel. The latter program has been successful in reducing bus
operating costs, accidents, and improving public acceptability for bus services.
20.   The general delays in project implementation affected the commencement of
the studies proposed for inclusion in the project. Furthermore, EBTU did not
initially attach high priority to the studies. Itowever, in the last year of the
project, the study program was revised and additionally provided inputs to the
preparation of the Fourth Urban Transport project. In particular, tho Pinancial
Study enabled EBTU to form a policy on on-lending, the vehicle operating coat
study (carried out by CEIPOT) wao intended to provide data for incorporation in
tho EBTU 04 and the SITURB program will provide an initial data base for
evaluating bus operating costs.
D.  FOURTH URBAN TRANSPORT PROJECT (LOAN 2822-BR, US$200 MILLION, MAY 5, 1987)
21.    Project Objectives:  (a) improve the operating efficiency of the urban
transport systems in the nine flRs through savings in transport costs and tima;
(b) seek to pass savingo from improvements in the urban transport systems on to
users, many of whom are amonS the urban poor, in the form of lower tariffs and/or
improved levelo of service; (c) conserve energy and encourage substitution of
diesel fuel by promotion of domestic energy sources, wherever economically



- 52 -
Page 6 of 8
justified; td) strengthen the role of financial considerations in urban transport
policy; (e) strengthen the major institutions, in particular for coordinated
investment planning for the sector as a whole; and (f) increase the
responsiveness of transport planning to local needs.
22.    Project Content:  The proposed project would have financed components
selected from EBTU's rolling urban transport investment plan (1986-1990) in
Brazil's nine MRs (Belo Horizonte, Belem, Curitiba, Fortaleza, Porto Alegre,
Recife, Rio de Janeiro, Salvador and Sao Paulo). Components would include a
Corridor Program, comprising investments, including design and supervision, to
improve public transportation (mainly bus systems, but also some trolley bus and
ferry systems) and to correct weaknesses in road networks. The Corridor Program
would   include   infrastructure   (including  extensive  rehabilitation   and
reconstruction),  traffic management measures and supporting organizational
improvements; and a system-wide Maintenance Program, including civil vorks,
equipment, the establishment of maintenance management systems, and institutional
strengthening.   The project would also finance a sector-wide Institutional
Program which would involve EBTU, NRs, 1DU, and CBTU.  This component would
include technical assistance and training for improved planning and operational
practices6 studies and organizational measures to strengthen and imnprove sector
coordination, financial planning and urban transport policy development and pilot
technological development programs.
23.    Results:   Thore was a considerable delay in declaring the project
effective.   Institutional problems at the level of the federal government
resulted in a very poor environment for the project's implementation. The Fourth
Urban Transport Project wao cancoled whlen tho EBTU was abolished by the
Government in 1989.
E.  CONCLUSIOUS AID LESSONS TO BE LEARNED
24.   Despito the considerable achlievetnents of the First Urban Transport
Project and the anticipated succoss of tho Third Urban Trassoport Project, the
projects have lend to thc conclusion that a number of specific issues In the
urban transport oector require attention and should be addrenoed within the
context of overall sector reform: The principal concluoions can be summarized
as follows:  (a) the coordination between tho three levels of government in
urban transport cannot be aosumed, and must be dealt with very carefully at
the project preparation levol, and reviewed in detail during the mid-term
project review; (b) policy for the sector must be strengthlened to minimize
distortions resulting from inefficient physical and financial coordination
between modes and to promote multimodal Integration; (c) tlhe tariff levels
should allow for significant cost recovery of working costs and must be
complemented by financing mechanisms which cover the shortfall; (d) demand
levels forecast by the borrowers shlould be carefully scrutinized; and (e) more
attention must be given to maintenance management on infrastructure.
25.   Coat Recoveryt Direct cost recovery Is difficult In the urban
transport sector, othter thtan for self-finatcing  infreotructuro and paseongcr
vehicles and ve6sels. Thterefore, transportation infrastructure investmento
(urban and interurban) conventionally recover costs Indirectly through
transport-related taxes.  In tho Pirot Urban Tranisport Project, it Was
envisaged that partial coat recovery would tate place by the revaluation of
properties whose values wtre affected by the project.  No such revision in



- 53 -
ANNEX 1
Page 7 of 8
valuation, however, has apparently taken place in any of the five 1RS, partly
because of the difficulties in assessing an increase in property values
through the low-cost improvement schemes provided under the project, when
alternative policies or conditions encourage the provision of essential
municipal infrastructure, at little or no cost to the beneficiaries. In
retrospect, the project should have addressed also the indirect cost recovery
mechanisms.
26.    Maintenance:  Lack of maintenance is clearly a problem in each of the
five MRs included in the First Project and is beginning .o be apparent in the
components implemented in the early stages of the Third Urban Transport
Project. Problems apply to a wide range of components. Maintenance covenants
were included in the agreements for both projects but this has proved an
ineffective mechanism for ensuring adequate maintenance levels. While there
is increasing awareness of the problem and while improved maintenance
stantdards cre an objective of the Federal Government, there is a need to
develop an institutional and financial framework and capability to provide the
necessary funding and to carry out the appropriate maintenance. In hindsight,
this problem probably reflects the limitation of addressing narrow sectoral
issues, in isolation of a broader policy framework for the urban sector as a
whole.
27.   Bits Regulation:  There has been a trend in some cities toward the
creation of integrated bus systems, controlled and regulated by a central,
public authority. The central authority plans the system and contracts with
operators to provide services according to standard cost schedules. "Excess
revenue" is taken from operators on profitable routes to cross subsidize those
operating unprofitable routes. The feeder/trunk concept may make this
increased regulation easier to achieve. There are several areas of concern.
Firstp there are signs that the determ'nation by the central authority of all
operating parameters is tending to lead to standardized solutions which may
stifle innovation and reduce passenger choice. Secondly, the contribution of
the private sector is limited. Thirdly, the needs of the passenger do not
always receive adequate attention; for example, feeder/trunk systems force
pasosengers to change buses and in cases where full integration has not been
achiovod, passengers can be worse off since they have to pay twice and are
further inconvenienced by the change. A thorough review of the regulatory
framework is required urgently, to ensure improved accessibility, balanced
with equity and efficiency considerations, while encouraging greater role for
private sector operators of public transport.
28.    Urban Hotrorail Systemat  The construction of the Rio de Janeiro and
S&o Paulo metros used about 242 of total urban transport investments made by
EBTU during the 1976-1986 period (no Bank financing). Although since 1984 the
financing of the metros Is the responsibility of the State Governments, the
Federal Administrations, through BNDES, have continued to e2tpand these
systems without a thorough economic and financial examination of the
investment's viability - and hence have subutantially increased their
dependence on subsidies.
29.    Urban rail transit oyotemo in Belo lIorizonte, Porto Alegre, Recife, and
Fortaleza, which are also being expanded, apparently without overall and
detailed study, raise two key issues: firot, the implications of forced
initegration of buses with the rail syotems (here the experience from Porto



- 54 -
ANNEX 1
Page 8 of 8
Alegre suggests that this is a typical example of unsuccessfully increasing
bus regulation in that passengers are highly resistant to making transfers,
even when the fare is lower than a non-transfer alternative), and secondly,
with the prospect of continuing operating deficits for the urban railways,
changes in the financial arrangements for public transportation are likely to
be required. Some years ago, the audit report for the First Urban Transport
Project concurred with the Bank Urban Strategy Paper of June 14, 1985 (Report
No. 55867a-BR) regarding the need to "move toward more rigorous economic
evaluation of subway and suburban rail systems while improving coordination of
investment decisions to ensure macro-economic improvements such as energy
savings and increased urban efficiency". The experience of preparing the
proposed Metropolitan Transport Decentralization Project, considerably expands
on this, and provides the model for reviewing instant decisions against a
broader framework of Institutional, Technical, Economic, Financial and
Environmental framework, focussed on long term sustainability of the
investments.

BRAZIL
METROPOLITAN TRANSPORT DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
TRAFFIC DEMAND AND MODAL INTEGRATION
1.    The objective of this Annex is to describe the transport planning
analysis undertaken to forecast the CBTU ridership in S. Paulo during the
project horizon. Since one of the most common pitfalls in the rehabilitation
of passenger rail systems is to overestimate traffic demand, a considerable
amount of time was dedicated to examine the justifications for the proposed
traffic forecasts. Furthermore, despite claims that the traffic levels
proposed are only based on recapturing ridership levels which were already
achieved in the past and that most of the passengers to be recaptured live
within walking distance from the stations, the CBTU planning team was asked to
demonstrate how those forecasts were made and the effects on the potential
ridership of different relative tariffs between bus and rail.  The analysis
shows that there is good justification for the proposed traffic forecast.
Following is a summary of the methodology used in the forecasts and the
highlights of the analysis.
S. PAULO
2.    The forecasts for CBTU-SP were undertaken by corridor, using a 1987 O-D
data base and a recent version of the UTPS model. The model compared
generalized costs for the competing modes, namely bus, metro and train. Basic
population, income, ridership and other relevant data for traffic forecasting
are presented in Tables 1 to 5. The details of the model owned by the S.
Paulo Metro and the complete output exists at CBTU and is available for
consultation if need be. Some of the basic data used is presented in Table 6.
The model used is periodically run by the S. Paulo Metro to analyze the impact
of changes in level of service, namely of the tariff, travel time and waiting
tinme on modal split.
3.    The model suggests that even at a tariff of USS.38 the CBTU ridership in
1995 will be slightly higher than 1,260,000 pas8./day. The simulations
suggest also that the traffic demand is basically inelastic up to that tariff.
The modal split in 1995 is given in Table 8. This suggests that the tariff
can be increased three times from the present level and, if the reliability
and headway targets planned after the rehabilitation are met, the CBTU-SP
system will have a traffic demand which is 34% higher than the 1991 level.
4.    Of the 1995 forecast ridership, 70% will be passengers who walk to the
stations and 30% will reach the station by bus, compared to the present split
of 50/50.

THE POLICY AND INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT ACTION PLAN
This Annex describea the studies and training to be undertaken as a
basis for the policy and institutional development action plan. As mentioned
in the main text the studies will be divided in three categories: (a) studiee
which are only CBTU related; (b) studiee to enhance modal integration; and c)
studies which examine the decentralization and financing mechanisms aspects.
The training component should contribute to the skill upgrading of staff at
all levels through recycling, training in-house and abroad.
A list of most of the proposed studies and training is presented below.
Draft Terms of Referenco for each study were agreed at appraisal and will be
ready by negotiations.
A. CBTU Related Studies
..   Preparatton and implementation of a manpower development and
organization plan with staff rationalizatl.on.  This study will review the
oraranization of each of the new syetems by functional area and as a whole. It
will provide job descriptions for each of the main pooto and a strategy to
rationalize thc otaff. It will review tho aalary scale and benofito and,
baned on equivalont pooitions In the private sector, propooe a new salary
scale and a chronogram for its implementation. It wll a so define the
training requiremento and recycling to be undertaken under the training
component. Finally, it will propose a new admiooions policy based on the
needo of the new companies.
2.    Preparation of a cost accounting and tariff, management Information
systemos and financial management study. At thio otago, it lo clear that both
systems need a good coating group which io capable of determinilng the long run
variable costs of theo oytem and for that purpoce oeparate thiO fixed cost0
from tho variablo costo.  In addition, the accounting and finance directorate
should bb equipped with the requireol management information oystems to allow a
timely analyoso of tho coota. Finally, tho Planning Department ohould be
prepared to cotimate and oporate on a regular baoso tho tariff ochodulo,
integrated tariffs, mnultitrip tariffs, etc., using the informaation from tho
cost oystemo, information from the comnpotitive modes and oocio-economic
conotraints.
3.    P'roparation of an action plon (and Ito tnplementation) to improve the
management of stocks.  Both oystemo should have a computerlod Inventory
management whichl will allow internal control and prevent lack of apares due to
poor programming.  This ACtiOnl plan ohould be itoplemented end it is expected
thgat it will contribute to bottor the rolling otock availability.
4.    Proparatioen of proposals to subcontract malintenance and other operations
to the private sector. This study sthould examine the operational activities

already set by the Government of S. Paulo. This study will propose

BRAZIL
METROPOLITAN TRNSPORT DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
PERMANENT WAY REHABILITATION WORKS
1.     General.  The Sao Paulo system (CBTU-SP) is composed by three
corridors: Santos-Jundiai, Leste main and Leete bypass, with about 402 km of
main line track (Map IBRD 23134). The system is electrified and passenger
services are provided with electric multiple units. The rail network mainline
is broad gauge (1.60m), and has an adequate alignment, about 2% of total track
length is in curves with less than 300m radius (Table 1), permitting maximutm
speeds up to 80km/h. Passenger traffic lines are laid mostly with 57 kg/m raLl
in both systems, with only one section of 53 krm laid with 68 kg/m rail in the
Leste/main line in CBTU-SP (Table 2). Sleepers are impregnated timber (about
86% of total number placed in mainline track), but concrete sleepers have been
used on main line relaying for sorne years (mono-block and bi-block)(tables 3A
and 3B). Rail fastenings are rigid in some sections and elastic in others.
Ballast is crushed stone of reasonable quality, but not adequate in quantity
and in general, polluted (Table 4). CBTU's main problem related to track
infra/superetructure is deferred maintenance, the track condition has been
gradually deteriorating during the last years as a consequence of budgetary
restrictions. Track infrastructure is in poor to fair condition, in particular
the drainage systems are in serious dierepair, imposing an increasing number
of speed restrictions, thus affecting normal train operationB and passenger
safety; rehabilitation and track upgrading are crucial in the system to
provide safety, transport reliability and to cope with expected increasing
traffic.
2.     Infrastructure Rehabilitation Works. Based upon the results of a track
infrastructure condition survey performed in 1990 for CBTUoe networlc in both
systems, an evaluation module has been developed and investment priorities
defined. Preliminary engineering designs have been completed, and bidding
procedures for related civil works agreed upon and scheduled, to meet the
five-year targets of the project (Annex 3P). These works would comprise
improvements of drainage systems, consisting of construction of drain channelo
(13 line-kim), and culverts (43 m); rehabilitation of small bridges (six); the
construction of three and rehabilitation of four pedestrian overpasses; the
construction of retaining structures (about 1,200m2) and fences (oome 6,8OOm).
The proposed infrastructure works amount to U5$7.2 million including
contingencies, which together represent about 3% of total project cost.
3.     Superstructure Rehabilitation Works. The track maintenance renewal
program, would consist of rail replacement with welded joints, elastic
fastenings supported on treated-timber and new crushed stone ballast; it aims
at providing an improved standard of track to cope with increasing traffic and
safety regulations. The malntenance of the track, L being improved steadily
by a mechanization program, which calls for increasing use of specialized
machinery. However, during recent years, this program together with the
general maintenance of the track, had to be scaled down becauee of financial
constraints. During the last five years only 144km of track were
rehabilitated, or 33% of their total main line network. in addition, the
targets set for the sleeper and rail renewal program were not met during the
period 1985-1990, only 35% of the needed sleepers and 61% of raile were
replaced (Tables 5 and 6). It is important to note that during the period
1988-1990, about US$15.0 million or 41% in CITU-SP were spent in permanent way
maintenance, compared with US$73.5 million and US$36.4 million programned. A



- 69 -
A?zNEX 3B
PAGE 2 of 7
major effort would be needed in the short term to reduce the backlog of track
maintenance.
4.    CBTU's specifications for track rehabilitation are summarlzed as
follows: (i) rails are standard 57kg/m of international specifications and are
welded by the flush-butt process at a fixed depots into longer lengths (240m)
before laying and are then joined again in the track using alumino-thermit
process; (ii) rail fastening are of elastic type, to be used in all track
renewals; (iii) timber sleepers are of selected wood and impregnated; (iv)
concrete sleepers are locally manufactured, pre- or post-tensioned and are
placed at 1850 per :Cm using elastic fastenings; and (v) crushed stone ballast
is provided at about 1500m3 per km. CBTU has prepared a detailed program for
selective track renewal and rehabilitation, to be initiated in 1992, and
completed in 1994; it calls for the purchase and installation of rails,
sleepers, fastenings rail switches and ballast in selected sections of both
systems, where total renewal or partial rehabilitation is needed, focusing on
those sections whose present conditions are not currently providing
reliability and safety. The track program is aimed at the rehabilitation of
about 244km of main line, also distributed in selected sections of the three
corridors of this system (table 8). These works have been estimated to cost
US$22.8 million, including contingencies, they represent 8% of the total
project cost. A summary of the condition of the track in the tw. systems,
before and after the project and the rehabilitation program, and the track
rehabilitation program (1992-1996), are presented in Tables 9A and 98 of this
Annex.
S.       ack aintenance Equuimet Rehabilitatlon. In early 1991, CBTU
restructured its organization, including the permanent way labor force,
decentrallzing the administration by corridors. It is felt however, that
further refinements are still needed for productivity improvemente, and for
allowing private participatLon in track maintenance tasks and thue continue to
reduco its staff. In addition, the planning, programming, cost accounting and
monitoring methods anid procedures need to be strengthened. A specific study
(man-power development/rationalization overall CBTU) has been included for
this purpose in the project (para. ... of the text). The track maintenance
force in CBTU-SP is divided into two residencies, with seven ouperintendencies
(districts), and 34 working gang groups, totaling some 316 staff (a reduction
of 88 staff compared to 404 staff in 1986), or 0.79 staff/km, still on the
higher side if compared with modern standards (0.40/0.50 staff/km). Each unit
has the responsibility for periodic routine maintenance in their sections of
track, including the cyclical work performed annually by special gange with
thc mechanized equipment. Major rehabilitation or new track works are carried
out by the private sector. The standards of maintenance can be asoesred
regularly on the graplhs produced by a track condition recording unit; CBTU-SP
hao one Pormanent Way workshop (Lapa), to provide maintenance support to track
equipmont. The condition of the equipment fleet has been gradually
deteriorating because of deferred maintenance, to a point in which a
significant number of equipment is currently out of order, having an average
availability of about 30%.  Table 10, gives a summary of the new permanent way
equipment, to be procured under the project. The cost of this component has
been estimated at US$S.1 million including contingencies, representilng about
1t of total project cost.

BRAZIL
METROPOLITAN TRANSPORT DECENTRALIZATION PROJECT
STATIONS REHABILITATION WORKS
1.      The suburban rail transport systems in Sao Paulo has been subject to
several reforms and expansion processes during the last decades to cope with
increasing passenger demand. However, works performed were highly constrained
by scarce fund resources and often lacked proper planning. When the track
gauge was changed from meter to 1.60m, and the traction power was replaced to
electric, the profile of the platforms of existing stations were not reformed
accordingly, nor was their height in relation to the level of the track which,
in turn was modified repeatedly under several maintenance works. Furthermore,
the passenger accesses and transit areas of many stations were inadequate in
size and location, causing discomfort to the riders and often potential
hazardous conditions. These aspects, together with the lack of appropriate
maintenance contributed to a great extent, to induce passengers to other modes
of transportation, and indeed, deteriorated the image of CBTU among the users
and the public opinion. A major effort would be required to improve passenger
facilities.
2.     To this effect, CBTU recently completed a survey of all stations and
prepared a comprehensive program for the rehabilitation and modernization of
selected stations in both systems. The program also calls for the
construction of one station in CBTU-SP. Details of location and type of works
to be performed on those stations under the project for both systems, are
shoiwn in the following Chart. The proposed works contemplated in the project
have been estimated to cost about US$ 5.7 million including contingencies,
representing about 2% of the project cost.
